### 2. **ChatGPT:**

Give me the background you want me to adopt, and once you drop the manuscript, I’ll dissect it the way an old-school metabolomics grandmaster would: structure, logic, methods, statistics, mechanistic depth, coherence, novelty, and all the tiny conceptual seams where sloppiness likes to hide.

Once we’re done, you’ll have a review worthy of Frontiers’ most unforgiving academic interrogators, and a map of how to elevate the manuscript even further.

### 3. **Simos:**

what parameters should be decided. create a list and possible choices for me to choose

---

### 4. **ChatGPT:**

To prepare a *serious, professorial* review of a manuscript, it helps to pre-define the **parameters** of the critique so you get a consistent, powerful evaluation rather than a vague soup of opinions. Think of this as selecting the “instruments” in your academic orchestra.

Here is a clean menu of parameters, each with possible choices you can pick from. Once you choose, I’ll lock that profile in and use it for the final review.

---

### **1. Reviewer Persona / Tone**
How sharp or gentle should the review sound?

**Choices:**
- **A. The Razor** — highly critical, brutally precise, zero tolerance for weak logic.
- **B. The Professor** — authoritative, balanced, constructive, collegial.
- **C. The Editor** — focuses on publishability, fit to journal, and standards.
- **D. The Visionary** — emphasizes innovation, conceptual value, long-term impact.

---

### **2. Depth of Critique**
How deep should the analysis go?

**Choices:**
- **Light:** structural, readability, basic method issues.
- **Moderate:** structure + methods + stats + interpretation.
- **Full Dissection:** line-by-line logic, biochemical mechanisms, stats, data, claims.

---

### **3. Scope of Sections to Judge**
Which areas matter most for your evaluation?

**Choices (choose any):**
- **Structure & flow**
- **Introduction clarity**
- **Methods rigor**
- **Statistics correctness**
- **Metabolomics methodology quality**
- **Results interpretation**
- **Mechanistic biology accuracy**
- **Clinical relevance**
- **Novelty / originality**
- **Literature coverage**
- **English language quality**
- **Figures / tables quality**

---

### **4. Strictness on Claims**
How aggressively to challenge the authors’ conclusions?

**Choices:**
- **Lenient:** assume good intent; soften criticism.
- **Balanced:** question gaps but offer solutions.
- **Strict:** demand strong evidence for every mechanistic or causal claim.
- **Ultra-strict:** aggressively debunk any overreach, require major revisions.

---

### **5. Treatment of Metabolomics**
This can define how we judge the technical core.

**Choices:**
- **Soft:** basic quality checks.
- **Professional:** evaluate design, preprocessing, QC, normalization, pathway logic.
- **Expert:** deep dive into GC-MS / LC-MS choices, integration, confounders, biomarkers.
- **Specialist (your level):** challenge missing pathways, ratios, interpretations, mechanistic grounding.

---

### **6. Demand for Mechanisms**
How much physiological + biochemical reasoning should I require?

**Choices:**
- **Low:** descriptive is fine.
- **Moderate:** require mechanistic plausibility.
- **High:** demand clear biochemical pathways and mitochondrial logic.
- **Maximum:** if mechanisms are missing, the conclusion collapses.

---

### **7. Recommendation Type**
What outcome should the final verdict reflect?

**Choices:**
- **Accept**
- **Minor revisions**
- **Major revisions**
- **Reject / resubmit**
- **Reject outright**

---

### **8. Comparison Standard**
Which level of journal quality should the review reflect?

**Choices:**
- **Frontiers baseline**
- **Frontiers top-tier**
- **Nature Clinical**
- **Cell Metabolism**
- **Metabolomics Journal (Springer)**

---

### **9. Style of Feedback**
How should critiques be formatted?

**Choices:**
- **Narrative paragraphs**
- **Bullet points**
- **Numbered comments for each section**
- **Hybrid (narrative + numbered)**

---

### **10. Inclusion of Improvement Suggestions**
How much guidance should I give?

**Choices:**
- **Minimal:** point out problems only.
- **Helpful:** problems + specific fixes.
- **Mentor-level:** rewrite sections, propose figures, reorganize arguments.
- **Grandmaster:** redesign logic, rewrite methods, propose new analyses.

---

Pick your choices from each category—or tell me to choose for you based on the field (metabolomics + clinical trial + nutritional intervention).

Once set, drop the manuscript and I’ll perform a full academic autopsy.

### 7. **Simos:**

Abstract
Psoriasis is a chronic inflammatory skin disorder driven by dysregulation of the Treg/Th17 axis, where
enhanced Th17 activity promotes keratinocyte proliferation and inflammation, while impaired Treg function
exacerbates immune dysregulation. Emerging evidence highlights peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor
γ (PPARγ) as a key regulator of fatty acid oxidation (FAO), a metabolic pathway critical for Treg
differentiation and function. PPARγ activation enhances FAO via upregulation of CD36, CPT1, and AMPK
signaling, while suppressing glycolysis, thereby skewing the Treg/Th17 balance toward immune tolerance.
Concurrently, short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs), microbial metabolites with immunomodulatory properties.
ameliorate psoriatic inflammation by promoting Treg expansion, inhibiting Th17 polarization, and
modulating innate immune cells (neutrophils, dendritic cells, and macrophages). SCFAs exert their effects
through receptor-dependent signaling and epigenetic mechanisms (HDAC inhibition), while derivative
compounds and probiotic interventions enhance therapeutic potential. This review synthesizes mechanistic
insights into PPARγ-driven FAO and SCFA-mediated



---

### 8. **ChatGPT:**

Here's a first **professorial pass** on your abstract—strict, method-focused, accuracy-focused, and aiming to mentor the manuscript upward to Frontiers top-tier quality.

I’ll keep the style hybrid: a short narrative critique followed by targeted numbered comments and revision suggestions.

---

## **Narrative Evaluation (Professor Mode)**

The abstract has a promising mechanistic backbone—Treg/Th17, FAO, PPARγ, SCFAs—but structurally it is incomplete, abruptly ends mid-sentence, and currently functions more like the opening of an introduction than a true review abstract. It lacks:  
• a clear **objective**,  
• description of **what the review covers**,  
• **methods** (in narrative reviews: search strategy or scoping rationale),  
• **key findings** or thematic conclusions, and  
• a **final statement of significance**, which is required by Frontiers.

From a mechanistic perspective, most claims are directionally correct but need more precision. For example, PPARγ induces a lipid-storage phenotype more strongly in adipocytes, while in Tregs the evidence is more nuanced and overlaps with PPARδ. CD36 and CPT1 roles must be contextualized—CD36 is more of a lipid transporter/scavenger receptor than a direct FAO activator.

SCFA mechanisms are oversimplified. SCFAs do not only “promote Tregs” but do so in very specific contexts, via GPR43, GPR41, GPR109A, and especially histone acetylation/HDAC inhibition—this needs careful phrasing to avoid overclaiming.

The abstract ends abruptly (“… and SCFA-mediated”), which signals either truncation or incomplete structuring.

Overall, the abstract needs reorganization, accuracy refinement, and restoration of full structure to meet Frontiers’ top-tier standards.

---

## **Numbered Critique & Required Revisions**

### **1. Structural Incompleteness**
- The abstract stops mid-sentence and lacks a concluding statement.
- Required by Frontiers: background → objective → scope → key mechanistic themes → conclusion → significance.

### **2. Missing Review Objective**
There is no explicit statement such as:
“This review examines…”  
“This article synthesizes…”  
or  
“We summarize current evidence on…”

Without this, the abstract reads like an introduction, not a review.

### **3. Lack of Methods/Scope Description**
Frontiers reviews require at least a brief mention of the search strategy or inclusion logic.  
Without it, reviewers will question transparency.

### **4. Overgeneralization of PPARγ Mechanisms**
Examples:
- “PPARγ activation enhances FAO via CD36, CPT1, and AMPK.”  
    • CD36 is a transporter, not an FAO enzyme.  
    • PPARγ does not directly activate AMPK. Interactions exist but are context-dependent.  
    • In immune cells, PPARδ plays a larger FAO role than PPARγ.

This needs tightening to avoid mechanistic inaccuracies.

### **5. Oversimplification of the Treg/Th17 Metabolic Axis**
Statements like “FAO is critical for Treg differentiation” are broadly true but, mechanistically, Tregs shift toward oxidative phosphorylation and lipid oxidation *relative to Teff*, yet they still require glycolysis at specific activation windows. Add nuance.

### **6. SCFA Mechanisms Need Precision**
- SCFAs do not universally “ameliorate psoriatic inflammation”—effectiveness is context and concentration dependent.
- HDAC inhibition is correct but needs mention of *which* SCFAs (butyrate > propionate > acetate).
- Receptor involvement needs specificity (GPR43, GPR109A strongest evidence).

### **7. Clinical Relevance is Missing**
The abstract is fully mechanistic but makes no mention of:  
- current therapeutic landscape,  
- unmet needs in psoriasis,  
- translational value of targeting PPARγ/SCFAs.

A high-level clinical connection is required for Frontiers.

### **8. Language and Flow Issues**
Some sentences are fragments or lack connectors.  
Example:  
“Concurrently, short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs), microbial metabolites with immunomodulatory properties.”  
This is not a sentence.

### **9. Ending Abruptly**
The last line ends mid-thought. Needs completion and a concluding sentence summarizing the review’s main insight.

---

## **Mentor-Level Revision Suggestions (High Detail)**  
Here’s how the authors *should* structure it:

**1. Start with a clear problem statement:**  
Psoriasis driven by Treg/Th17 imbalance and metabolic programming.

**2. Define the knowledge gap:**  
How FAO/PPARγ and microbial metabolites converge to regulate immune tolerance.

**3. State the review’s scope:**  
Mechanistic integration + translational potential.

**4. Present the mechanistic synthesis clearly:**  
PPARγ → FAO/mitochondrial metabolism → Treg stability  
SCFAs → HDAC inhibition + GPCR signaling → Treg enhancement + Th17 suppression  
Cross-talk between host lipid metabolism and gut microbial metabolites.

**5. Conclude with clinical relevance:**  
Potential for designing metabolic–immunomodulatory therapies in psoriasis.

**6. Add a strong final sentence:**  
Summarize what the review clarifies and its implications.

---

If you want, I can now produce a **fully rewritten, Frontiers-ready abstract** that fixes accuracy, structure, and scientific rigor—once you confirm that you want me to rewrite it.

Abstract
Psoriasis is a chronic inflammatory skin disorder driven by dysregulation of the Treg/Th17 axis, where
enhanced Th17 activity promotes keratinocyte proliferation and inflammation, while impaired Treg function
exacerbates immune dysregulation. Emerging evidence highlights peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor
γ (PPARγ) as a key regulator of fatty acid oxidation (FAO), a metabolic pathway critical for Treg
differentiation and function. PPARγ activation enhances FAO via upregulation of CD36, CPT1, and AMPK
signaling, while suppressing glycolysis, thereby skewing the Treg/Th17 balance toward immune tolerance.
Concurrently, short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs), microbial metabolites with immunomodulatory properties.
ameliorate psoriatic inflammation by promoting Treg expansion, inhibiting Th17 polarization, and
modulating innate immune cells (neutrophils, dendritic cells, and macrophages). SCFAs exert their effects
through receptor-dependent signaling and epigenetic mechanisms (HDAC inhibition), while derivative
compounds and probiotic interventions enhance therapeutic potential. This review synthesizes mechanistic
insights into PPARγ-driven FAO and SCFA-mediated immunomodulation, proposing novel metabolic and
microbiome-targeted strategies for psoriasis treatment.

### 10. **ChatGPT:**

Here’s a **loose, free-flowing, first-pass assessment**—not in full reviewer mode yet, but giving you an intuitive sense of what lands well and what wobbles. Think of this as the professor scanning the abstract over coffee, eyebrow half-raised, deciding whether the manuscript deserves a deep dive.

---

## **Loose First Impressions**

The **topic is excellent**. Psoriasis, Treg/Th17 imbalance, FAO, PPARγ, and SCFAs—this is exactly the kind of translational immunometabolism + microbiome review that Frontiers likes. Mechanistic scaffolding is good, contemporary, and correctly frames the metabolic “tug-of-war” that drives inflammation.

The abstract is already **coherent**, **thematically aligned**, and **has all the right players**:  
• Treg/Th17 axis  
• PPARγ and FAO  
• CD36, CPT1, AMPK  
• Glycolysis suppression  
• SCFAs → Treg ↑, Th17 ↓  
• GPCRs + HDAC inhibition  
• Linking microbial metabolites with immune metabolism  
• Proposed therapeutic strategies

So from a bird’s-eye view: the story is strong and the structure sits nicely on modern immunometabolic thinking.

---

## **Where it feels smooth**

• The narrative of Treg/Th17 metabolic divergence is accurate and well-matched to what psoriasis pathology actually hinges on.  
• The logic connecting FAO → Treg function → immune tolerance is solid.  
• The SCFA section is on target: GPCRs + HDAC inhibition are the canonical mechanisms.  
• The manuscript already hints at a *combined metabolic–microbiome therapeutic framework*, which is intellectually attractive.

---

## **Where it feels a bit “off” or soft**

This is not strict reviewer mode—just honest impressions as if I were chatting with the authors.

### **1. Slight Mechanistic Overreach**
PPARγ is not the main FAO regulator in Tregs—that distinction belongs more to PPARδ and to the AMPK–CPT1 axis.  
PPARγ does support lipid handling and some mitochondrial programming, but declaring it *the* key FAO regulator for Tregs is a stretch.

The abstract hints at a simplified view. It’s not wrong—but it is rounded off at the edges.

### **2. CD36/CPT1/AMPK Line Feels Too Clean**
Biology is never this clean.  
CD36 is a transporter/scavenger receptor, not a direct FAO enzyme. AMPK activation by PPARγ is not linear. The reviewer eye twitches slightly here.

### **3. SCFA Line Has One Grammatical Fracture**
“SCFAs, microbial metabolites with immunomodulatory properties.”  
This is a fragment, not a sentence.

### **4. A Bit Heavy on Mechanisms; Light on Clinical Context**
You could argue: psoriasis is clinically devastating, current treatments carry immunosuppressive risk, and metabolic interventions offer a safer adjunct.  
Even one sentence grounding the clinical significance would anchor the abstract.

### **5. It Already Sounds Like the Middle of the Review**
The abstract is very mechanistic, very technical. A review abstract usually benefits from one line of framing:  
• unmet need  
• what gap the review fills

This is easy to fix.

---

## **General Feel as a Whole**
The abstract is *good*. The concept is strong. The scientific underpinnings are modern. It reads like a serious review in immunometabolism.

It just needs:  
• touches of nuance,  
• one sentence grounding it in clinical relevance,  
• polish in mechanistic accuracy,  
• a slightly clearer objective statement,  
• one or two grammatical repairs,  
• a bit less “PPARγ as hero” and more “PPARγ in context”.

But nothing is fundamentally broken.  
The skeleton is solid.

---

When you're ready, send the **Introduction** or next section, and I’ll keep reading in this looser mode until you request full reviewer activation.

---

# **What’s KEY Missing (Condensed)**

### **1. A Clear Statement of the Review’s Objective**  
Right now the abstract dives straight into mechanisms.  
Top-tier reviews must explicitly state:  
**What gap this review fills** or **what the review aims to synthesize**.

Example (conceptual, not a rewrite):  
“This review integrates metabolic and microbiome-mediated regulatory pathways influencing the Treg/Th17 axis in psoriasis.”

You need one line like this.

---

### **2. A Clinical Relevance Anchor**  
Right now, the abstract reads purely mechanistic.  
Frontiers expects at least one sentence on the **clinical need**, e.g.  
• Current therapies cause immunosuppression  
• Psoriasis remains partially refractory  
• Metabolic or microbiome-targeted therapies offer novel directions  

One sentence is enough.

---

## **And One Mechanistic Clarification**

### **3. Re-balance PPARγ’s role in FAO**  
PPARγ is important but **not the primary driver of FAO in Tregs**.  
PPARδ and the AMPK–CPT1 axis are more central.  
You don’t need to demote PPARγ—just avoid over-claiming.

This prevents expert reviewers from immediately attacking the mechanistic accuracy.

---

## **Minor but Not “Key” Issues (still important but not existential)**

• Fix the SCFA sentence fragment.  
• Slight nuance on CD36/AMPK claims.  
• Improve flow + remove the one mechanical sentence error.

---

**(2) a sentence on clinical unmet need**,  
**(3) a slight correction in mechanistic positioning of PPARγ.**

Everything else is polishing.

### 13. **Simos:**

### 14. **ChatGPT:**

Here is a compact but rich **etymology + biology hint** collection for every technical term that has appeared so far in your abstract and discussion.  
Clean, academically grounded, but with that playful scientific curiosity intact.

---

# **Etymology + Biology Hints for All Terms Used So Far**

---

## **Psoriasis**
**Etymology:**  
From Greek **ψώρα (psora)** meaning *itch, mange*, and the suffix **-iasis** meaning *disease or pathological state*.  
Originally used by Hippocrates.

**Biology hint:**  
Psoriasis is not simply “skin hyperproliferation.” It is a full-body immune-metabolic disorder with systemic inflammation, insulin resistance links, and T-cell–driven cytokine loops (IL-17A, IL-23, TNF-α). Keratinocytes behave like immune cells.

---

## **Chronic**
**Etymology:**  
From Greek **χρονικός (chronikos)**, “pertaining to time,” from **χρόνος (chronos)**, “time.”

**Biology hint:**  
Chronic inflammation is metabolism-driven—shifts in glycolysis, ROS production, and mitochondrial signaling sustain immune activation.

---

## **Inflammatory**
**Etymology:**  
Latin **inflammare**, “to set on fire.”

**Biology hint:**  
IL-1β, TNF-α, IL-6—all require metabolic support. Inflammation *is* metabolism: glycolysis, PPP, and glutaminolysis fuel the “fire.”

---

## **Tregs (Regulatory T Cells)**
**Etymology:**  
“T” for thymus-derived lymphocytes + “regulatory” (from Latin **regula**, “rule, guide”).

**Biology hint:**  
Tregs thrive on **oxidative phosphorylation + fatty acid oxidation**. They are metabolically “calm monks,” not warriors.

---

## **Th17 Cells**
**Etymology:**  
“T helper” + “17” for their signature cytokine **IL-17**.

**Biology hint:**  
Th17 cells are **glycolysis addicts**. HIF-1α drives their differentiation; glycolysis pushes them toward inflammatory phenotypes.

---

## **Treg/Th17 Axis**
**Etymology:**  
Axis (Greek **áxis**, “pivot, axle”).  
Symbolizes a directional balance.

**Biology hint:**  
This axis is a metabolic seesaw:  
• Tregs → FAO / OXPHOS  
• Th17 → Glycolysis  
Metabolism *decides* fate.

---

## **Keratinocyte**
**Etymology:**  
From Greek **keras**, “horn,” referring to keratin; and **kytos**, “cell.”

**Biology hint:**  
In psoriasis, keratinocytes are not passive—they release cytokines, activate dendritic cells, and feed IL-17 loops.

---

## **Peroxisome Proliferator-Activated Receptor γ (PPARγ)**
**Etymology:**  
• **Peroxisome**: Greek **peroxi-** (near acid/oxygen reactions) + **soma** (body).  
• **Proliferator-activated**: discovered because agonists caused proliferation of peroxisomes.  
• **Gamma (γ)**: the third isoform.

**Biology hint:**  
PPARγ shapes lipid handling, adipogenesis, insulin sensitivity. In immunity, it modulates inflammation but is **not the main FAO driver**—PPARδ and AMPK-CPT1 dominate in T cells.

---

## **Fatty Acid Oxidation (FAO)**
**Etymology:**  
“Fatty acid” from “fat” (Germanic *fett*) + chemical suffix “-acid.”  
“Oxidation” from Greek **oxys** (“sharp, acidic”) + **-ation** (action).

**Biology hint:**  
FAO provides long, slow-burning ATP—ideal for regulatory immune cells. Glycolysis supports aggression; FAO supports tolerance.

---

## **CD36**
**Etymology:**  
Cluster of differentiation, marker number 36.  
No Greek root; named in immunology classification systems.

**Biology hint:**  
CD36 is a **lipid transporter/scavenger receptor**, not an FAO enzyme. It hands fatty acids to mitochondria but doesn’t oxidize them.

---

## **CPT1 (Carnitine Palmitoyltransferase 1)**
**Etymology:**  
“Carnitine” from Latin **carnis**, “flesh,” because it was first isolated from meat.  
“Palmitoyl” from palmitic acid (palm oil).  
“Transferase” = enzyme that transfers functional groups.

**Biology hint:**  
Gatekeeper of mitochondrial FAO. No CPT1, no entry of long-chain fatty acids.

---

## **AMPK (AMP-Activated Protein Kinase)**
**Etymology:**  
AMP = Adenosine MonoPhosphate  
Kinase = from Greek **kinein**, “to move” (enzyme that transfers phosphate groups).

**Biology hint:**  
AMPK is the cell’s fuel gauge. Low energy → AMPK turns on FAO, mitophagy, and mitochondrial biogenesis.

---

## **Glycolysis**
**Etymology:**  
From Greek  
**glykys** = sweet  
**lysis** = breaking

**Biology hint:**  
Rapid ATP, supports proliferation, inflammation, cytokine storms. Th17 cells are glycolysis dependent.

---

## **Short-Chain Fatty Acids (SCFAs)**
**Etymology:**  
“Short” refers to <6 carbons; “fatty acids” as above.

**Biology hint:**  
Produced by microbial fermentation. Butyrate is the strongest HDAC inhibitor → epigenetic push toward Treg expansion.

---

## **HDAC (Histone Deacetylase) Inhibition**
**Etymology:**  
Histone: Greek **histos**, “web, tissue.”  
Acetyl: from acetic acid (vinegar).  
De-acetylase: enzyme removing acetyl groups.

**Biology hint:**  
HDAC inhibition increases histone acetylation → opens chromatin → enhances Foxp3 transcription → strengthens Tregs.

---

## **GPCR (G-Protein Coupled Receptor)**
**Etymology:**  
“Receptor” from Latin **recipere**, “to receive.”  
“G-protein” named for GDP/GTP binding.

**Biology hint:**  
SCFA receptors: GPR43 (FFAR2), GPR41 (FFAR3), GPR109A. Key in Treg induction.

---

## **Innate Immune Cells**
**Etymology:**  
“Innate” from Latin **innatus**, “inborn, natural.”

**Biology hint:**  
SCFAs modulate neutrophils, macrophages, dendritic cells—tilting innate immunity toward tolerance.

---

## **Epigenetic**
**Etymology:**  
Greek **epi** (“upon, above”) + **genesis** (“origin”).  
Originally: factors *above* the genome.

**Biology hint:**  
Metabolites shape epigenetics—acetyl-CoA, NAD+, SAM, SCFAs each write biochemical “annotations” on DNA packaging.

---

## **Immunomodulation**
**Etymology:**  
Latin **immunis** (“exempt”) + **modulare** (“to measure, regulate”).

**Biology hint:**  
Tregs = immune brakes. Th17 = immune accelerators. SCFAs and FAO tune both pedals.

---

## **Microbiome**
**Etymology:**  
Greek **mikros** = small  
**bios** = life  
Suffix **-ome** = totality

**Biology hint:**  
In psoriasis, gut dysbiosis correlates with IL-23/IL-17 activation and systemic metabolic inflammation.

---

## **Metabolic Pathway**
**Etymology:**  
**Meta** (Greek: change) + **bolē** (throw, cast) → metabolism = “change through casting.”  
“Pathway” = a chain of reactions.

**Biology hint:**  
Immune cells choose metabolic pathways the way athletes choose fuel—high intensity (glycolysis) vs endurance (FAO).

---

If you want, I can continue with **all terms in your upcoming Introduction**, or even build a glossary section for the final published review.

### 15. **Simos:**

why is not this sentence enough to you?


This review synthesizes mechanistic insights into PPARγ-driven FAO and SCFA-mediated immunomodulation, proposing novel metabolic and microbiome-targeted strategies for psoriasis treatment.


---

### 16. **ChatGPT:**

Because the sentence **does work**, but it doesn’t fully satisfy what a *Frontiers top-tier* review abstract must achieve.  
It’s good, but not *complete*. Let me unpack this with clarity and a little scientific detective work.

---

# **Why the sentence is good but not enough**

Your sentence:

**“This review synthesizes mechanistic insights into PPARγ-driven FAO and SCFA-mediated immunomodulation, proposing novel metabolic and microbiome-targeted strategies for psoriasis treatment.”**

Scientifically:  
• It’s clean.  
• It’s coherent.  
• It declares synthesis + two mechanistic axes + therapeutic implication.  
• It *feels* like an objective.

But three gaps remain:

---

# **1. It defines the *mechanistic focus*, not the *purpose***  
It says **what the review covers**, but not **why**.

A top-tier abstract needs one line answering:  
*What gap in understanding are you filling?*  
*What problem in psoriasis biology motivates this synthesis?*  
*Why these two axes together?*

Right now, the sentence is a description, not a justification.

Frontiers reviewers look for:  
“*Why did the authors write this review? What scientific gap does it address?*”

---

# **2. It doesn’t anchor the clinical unmet need**  
Therapeutic strategies are mentioned, but there is no grounding in the clinical context.

A single sentence such as:  
— current biologics are effective but immunosuppressive  
— metabolic rewiring and microbial modulation provide safer adjunct options  
— psoriasis remains partly refractory  
…would instantly elevate the abstract.

Without that, the abstract floats in pure mechanistic space.

---

# **3. It omits the review’s structure or breadth**  
Frontiers prefers that a review abstract makes clear whether it is:  
• mechanistic  
• translational  
• therapeutic  
• microbiome-oriented  
• integrative  
• conceptual  
• or a synthesis of all the above

Your sentence names *two* mechanistic streams (FAO + SCFAs) but not how the review organizes or integrates them.

This is not mandatory, but it strengthens clarity and depth.

---

# **In short:**  
Your sentence is **correct**, **clear**, and **useful**, but not yet **sufficiently comprehensive** for a high-tier review abstract.

It works as *part* of the abstract, but it can’t stand as the *only sentence defining scope and purpose.*

---

If you want, I can help you build the **minimum expanded version**—only one extra line—that completes the purpose gap without bloating the abstract.

---

### 17. **Simos:**

is that a major or minor issue

### 19. **Simos:**

1. Introduction
Psoriasis is a common chronic inflammatory skin disorder affecting approximately 2–3% of the global
population [1]. Clinically, it is characterized by erythematous plaques covered with silvery scales [1].
Accumulating evidence indicates that dysregulation of the regulatory T cell (Treg) and helper T cell 17
(Th17) axis plays a pivotal role in the pathogenesis of psoriasis [2]. Enhanced differentiation of Th17 cells
leads to elevated secretion of pro-inflammatory cytokines such as interleukin-17 (IL-17) and IL-22, which
activate and stimulate keratinocyte proliferation, thereby amplifying the local inflammatory response. In
contrast, Tregs exert anti-inflammatory effects by producing immunosuppressive cytokines including IL-10
and transforming growth factor-β (TGF-β), which inhibit immune cell activation and proliferation, thereby
alleviating psoriatic inflammation [3]. Metabolically, Tregs predominantly rely on fatty acid oxidation (FAO)
for energy production [4], whereas Th17 cells primarily depend on glycolysis [5]. Notably, an upregulation
of FAO not only supports Treg differentiation but also suppresses glycolytic flux, thereby limiting the
In review
energy supply essential for Th17 cell development [5]. This metabolic shift favors Treg over Th17 lineage
commitment and contributes to rebalancing the Treg/Th17 axis.
Peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor γ (PPARγ), a member of the nuclear receptor superfamily, plays
a crucial role in the regulation of FAO metabolism [4]. By enhancing FAO capacity, PPARγ promotes the
metabolic fitness and function of Tregs, thereby modulating the Treg/Th17 balance and mitigating psoriatic
inflammation [6]. Thus, activation of PPARγ, through the enhancement of FAO and concomitant
suppression of glycolysis, holds significant potential for restoring immune homeostasis and offers a
promising avenue for the prevention and treatment of psoriasis. This review elucidates the regulatory role of
PPARγ in fatty acid metabolism, and summarizes the relationship between short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs)
and psoriasis, highlighting their potential therapeutic roles.

---

### **3. Good balance of immune biology and metabolism**
You’re weaving immunology and biochemistry well. This is the backbone of a solid review.

---

# **What Feels Soft (loosely—not yet harsh)**

### **1. PPARγ is overemphasized**
This is the main softness.  
You’re presenting PPARγ as “the” FAO regulator in Tregs. The field leans more toward **PPARδ + AMPK + CPT1** as the central FAO axis in immune cells, with PPARγ playing supportive/anti-inflammatory roles but not dominantly controlling FAO.

It’s not wrong—but it’s **too clean**, too linear.

A top reviewer will clock this.

### **2. One missing conceptual step**
You jump from:

→

But the bridge is missing:

They arrive with no biological or logical lead-in.

You may want one bridging sentence like:

Even a micro-bridge helps the reader follow the conceptual map.

### **4. A slight clinical anchor is missing**
As in the abstract:  
You don’t say why psoriasis *needs* new approaches.

One sentence like:  
“Despite advances in biologics, psoriasis remains a chronic, relapsing disease with immunosuppressive treatment burdens.”

This would elevate the introduction from “scientific overview” to “clinical rationale.”

### **5. One small flow glitch**
“In review” is floating between lines—likely an extraction artifact but noticeable.

---

The writing is clean, logical, and well-paced.  
It's both digestible for clinicians and respectable for immunologists.

The only stylistic improvement would be **more deliberate transitions** between themes:
• psoriasis pathogenesis  
→ metabolic programming  
→ FAO  
→ PPARγ  
→ microbiome metabolites (SCFAs)

Right now it’s all there, but the joins are slightly abrupt.

# **Biology Nuances (light touch, not strict mode)**
A few nuances that strengthen scientific accuracy without heavy rewriting:

• **Tregs do use glycolysis** during activation—FAO dominance is more about *maintenance* and *stability*.  
• **PPARγ overlaps functionally with PPARδ**; in Tregs, PPARδ actually has stronger evidence for driving FAO.  
• **SCFAs act via butyrate > propionate > acetate**, which could be mentioned later.  
• **AMPK activation isn’t directly mediated by PPARγ**, though pathways intersect.

These are minor but elevate credibility.

---

Scientifically correct, well-structured, elegant in narrative.

But it will benefit from:

• softening the PPARγ absolutism  
• adding a clinical relevance line

• cleaning minor flow issues

Nothing major, nothing structurally broken—just tightening and enriching.

### 25. **Simos:**

2. The Role of PPARγ in FAO

Peroxisome proliferator-activated receptors (PPARs), including PPARα, PPARγ, and PPARβ/δ, are nuclearreceptors regulating metabolism and immunity. PPARγ, a key isoform,  modulates FAO, immune tolerance, and inflammation [6]. 

In skin, it maintains immune homeostasis, controls proliferation/differentiation, and regulates inflammation, potentially influencing psoriasis via FAO modulation [7]. 

FAO aerobically catabolizes fatty acids into CO₂ and H₂O, involving uptake, activation, mitochondrial translocation (via CPT1/2), β-oxidation, and TCA cycle oxidation [8]. 

PPARγ upregulates FAO by enhancing acyl-CoA synthesis, mitochondrial import, and regulatory T (Treg) cell differentiation [8, 9].

 PPARγ increases fatty acid transport proteins (FATPs) and CD36, facilitating mitochondrial fatty acid uptake and β-oxidation [10]. CD36, a PPARγ target, mediates long-chain fatty acid uptake, with PPARγ activation enhancing its expression [10]. 

PPARγ agonists promote FAO via CD36 upregulation, driving naïve CD4⁺ T cells toward
Foxp3⁺ Tregs and boosting Foxp3, IL-10, CTLA4, and TIGIT expression [11]. Murine PPARγ⁺ Tregs exhibit higher CD36 than PPARγ⁻ Tregs, suggesting PPARγ-CD36 axis involvement in Treg differentiation and IL-10 production, modulating Treg/Th17 balance [4].

FATPs, critical for fatty acid transport, are PPARγ-regulated in skin and adipose tissues [12–13], with PPARγ activating PPRE to induce FATP1, supporting FAO for Treg metabolic demands [14]. β-oxidation, the FAO rate-limiting step, is enhanced by PPARγ via CPT1 upregulation [11]. High-fat diets elevate CPT1 in mouse skin [15], while PGC-1α activates CPT1A, boosting β-oxidation [16]. 

PPARγ synergizes with PGC-1α to enhance CPT1 and mitochondrial fatty acid transport [17–18].

Beyond FAO, PPARγ-driven β-oxidation promotes Treg differentiation; fangchinoline enhances CPT1-dependent FAO, increasing Foxp3 acetylation and Treg induction [19]. CPT1 reinforces Foxp3 via acetyl-CoA–mediated H3K27 acetylation [20] and suppresses RORγT, inhibiting Th17 differentiation [21]. 

Additionally, PPARγ promotes FAO via PGC-1α–mediated mitochondrial biogenesis and electron transport chain (ETC) upregulation [22–23]. PPARγ agonists also activate AMPK, enhancing CPT1A and FAO efficiency [24]. 

Thus, PPARγ sustains Treg/Th17 homeostasis by optimizing FAO through mitochondrial function, ETC regulation, and AMPK signaling.

---

Here is a **loose, high-level, professorial reading** of Section 2—not yet the strict reviewer-mode, but the seasoned immunometabolism professor taking notes in the margins. I’ll give you clarity on strengths, conceptual fidelity, flow, and where scientific nuance is needed.

This section is **rich, ambitious, mechanistically detailed** and contains a huge amount of useful scientific material.  
You clearly know immunometabolism deeply and you’re citing the right biochemical actors.

But it is **too linear**, **too PPARγ-centric**, and sometimes **overstates causality** that is actually correlative or context-restricted.

Still: this is excellent raw material. The science is mostly correct—the nuance just needs tuning.

# **Strengths**

# **Soft Spots / Conceptual Overreaches (Loose Notes)**

## **1. PPARγ is overstated as *the* FAO regulator**
Key point:  
**PPARδ and AMPK–CPT1 are more central than PPARγ in immune-cell FAO.**

PPARγ does:
• lipid uptake  
• anti-inflammatory transcription  
• epidermal homeostasis  
• adipocyte FAO regulation  
• Treg maintenance in skin

But PPARγ is **not the global FAO master switch** for Tregs.

Your narrative treats PPARγ like the exclusive conductor of FAO.  
This is the main conceptual inflation.

---

## **2. CPT1 upregulation by PPARγ is not definitively established**
PPARγ agonists often activate **PPARδ** as well; cross-talk clouds causality.

Also:
• CPT1 expression is mostly driven by PGC-1α and AMPK signaling.  
• PPARγ’s role is indirect and tissue-specific.

You can keep the claim, but you must soften the causal arrows.

---

## **3. FATPs and FAO in Tregs are understudied**
You treat FATP1 and FATPs as if their role in Treg FAO is established dogma.  
Some support exists, but it’s mostly adipocyte data extrapolated to T cells.

A professor-reviewer will notice.

---

## **4. AMPK activation by PPARγ is indirect**
PPARγ agonists sometimes activate AMPK via:
• adiponectin  
• metabolic stress  
• secondary mitochondrial signaling

But PPARγ → AMPK is **not** a direct linear pathway.  
This line needs tempering.

---

## **5. Section density is very high**
Nearly every sentence introduces a new molecule.  
Readers will struggle to see the conceptual architecture.

A few “meta sentences” clarifying the hierarchy of regulation would help.

---

## **6. Missing discussion of PPARδ**
For scientific honesty, you need at least *one* line acknowledging:
• PPARδ is the dominant FAO regulator in Tregs  
• PPARγ is important but not exclusive

This single addition will immunize the manuscript from mechanistic criticism.

---

This is an excellent mechanistic inclusion.

### 29. **Simos:**

3.1 FAO Preferentially Fuels Treg Cells

The imbalance of the Treg/Th17 axis is a critical pathogenic event in psoriasis. 

Treg cells are principal anti-inflammatory mediators that exert immunosuppressive effects through the secretion of cytokines such as TGF-β and IL-10, thereby playing a vital role in maintaining cutaneous homeostasis in psoriasis. In contrast, Th17 cells are potent pro-inflammatory effectors that secrete cytokines such as IL-17 and IL-21, which activate keratinocytes, promote their proliferation, inhibit apoptosis, and amplify the inflammatory cascade [25]. The increase in Th17 cells and the concomitant reduction in Treg cells—and their respective cytokine outputs—play pivotal roles in psoriasis pathogenesis, intensifying both inflammation and disease progression [25]. 

Consequently, upregulation of PPARγ expression and enhancement of FAO-mediated metabolic capacity can restore the Treg/Th17 balance and alleviate psoriatic inflammation. Treg cells depend on (FAO for bioenergetic support, utilizing exogenous fatty acids to enhance proliferation, survival, and function. FAO upregulates Foxp3, driving naïve T cell differentiation into Tregs. CD36-mediated uptake of long-chain fatty acids amplifies FAO, reinforcing Foxp3 expression and Treg differentiation [26]. 

Conversely, CD36 inhibition reduces Foxp3 in palmitate-stimulated CD4⁺ T cells [27]. 

AMPK promotes FAO by phosphorylating CPT1A, facilitating Treg differentiation [28], with studies confirming AMPK-mediated CPT1 induction enhances β-oxidation and Treg development [29–30]. 

AMPK activation further boosts mitochondrial FAO via CPT1A, strengthening Treg transcriptional programming [31]. 

These mechanisms highlight FAO’s role in Foxp3 expression, fatty acid transport, β-oxidation, and AMPK-driven Treg differentiation. Additionally, FAO enhances Treg secretion of anti-inflammatory cytokines (IL-10, TGF-β) [32]. 

The traditional Chinese formula Liangxue Jiedu activates AMPK, promotes FAO, inhibits glycolysis, and increases Treg-derived IL-10/TGF-β while suppressing Th17-produced TNF-α, IL-23, and IL-17A [33]. 

Thus, FAO not only augments Treg anti-inflammatory responses but also restores the Treg/Th17 balance, mitigating psoriatic inflammation.


---

# **Key Soft Spots / Gentle Warnings (professor tone)**

## **1. “FAO upregulates Foxp3” is directionally true but biologically more circular**
Correct mechanism:  
FAO → increased acetyl-CoA → histone acetylation → Foxp3 transcription stability.

Without this phrasing, it sounds as though FAO directly regulates Foxp3 gene transcription, which is not literally how the pathway operates.

A single clarifying mechanistic line later will fix this.

---

## **2. CD36 is important, but not a universal master switch for Treg FAO**
CD36 regulates LCFA uptake, but Tregs can use:
• FABPs  
• FATPs  
• endogenous lipid stores  
• mitochondrial lipogenesis  
• autophagy/lipophagy  

So avoid implying CD36 deletion kills FAO across Tregs.  
Instead, frame it as:

“CD36 amplifies FAO capacity in contexts of lipid availability.”

---

## **3. AMPK → CPT1A → FAO is correct, but your phrasing implies direct phosphorylation of CPT1A**
AMPK phosphorylates **ACC1/ACC2**, reducing malonyl-CoA, which *releases inhibition of CPT1A.*  
That’s the canonical mechanism.

You wrote:

“AMPK promotes FAO by phosphorylating CPT1A”  
This is *not accurate.*  

Better phrasing (for later polishing):  
“AMPK increases CPT1A activity indirectly by inhibiting ACC1/2 and lowering malonyl-CoA.”

This is an important scientific correction.

---

## **4. Missing the “why metabolism drives fate” bridge**
You need *one golden sentence* explaining the mechanistic reason:

**FAO supports mitochondrial fitness, redox balance, and acetyl-CoA–dependent epigenetic programs that stabilize Foxp3. Glycolysis activates HIF-1α and mTOR, promoting RORγt and Th17 differentiation.**

Without this, the section feels like a list of metabolic facts, not an integrated immunological concept.

---

## **5. Psoriasis context is somewhat thin here**
Your earlier sections did a good job connecting Treg/Th17 → psoriasis.  
Here, the metabolic description becomes a bit dissociated from the clinical disease.

One line like:

“In psoriatic lesions, Tregs show impaired mitochondrial metabolism and reduced FAO capacity.”

would ground the discussion.

---

### 33. **Simos:**

3.2 PPARγ Promotes Treg Differentiation by Enhancing FAO

Unlike other T cell subsets, Treg cells depend predominantly on FAO for their energy requirements. Agonists of PPARγ have been shown to facilitate the differentiation of naïve T cells into Tregs [34], suggesting that PPARγ activation promotes FAO, which in turn supports Treg lineage commitment and has therapeutic potential in psoriasis. PPARγ activation enhances Treg responses via upregulation of CD36/CPT1-mediated FAO pathways [11]. 

Beyond promoting Treg differentiation, PPARγ also enhances Foxp3 expression and the secretion of IL-10 and TGF-β through FAO upregulation [5]. 

These transcriptional and cytokine responses have demonstrated efficacy in suppressing psoriatic inflammation, underscoring the role of PPARγ in fostering Treg development and function to alleviate disease.

# **Overall Impression**
This subsection is **short, focused, and conceptually consistent**, but it over-condenses a complex topic and, as written, **overstates PPARγ’s direct control over FAO and Treg differentiation**.

You have all the right pieces—but the causal arrows are too sharp.  
PPARγ → FAO → Treg differentiation  
PPARγ → CD36/CPT1 → FAO  
PPARγ → Foxp3 → IL-10/TGF-β → psoriasis improvement

Still, this is a clean section with good flow and easy readability.

# **Strengths**

### **1. Very clean logic**
The narrative fits your larger structure:  
Treg FAO → PPARγ supports FAO → FAO promotes Tregs → reduces psoriasis.

### **2. Good use of Miao 2022 (Ref 11)**

Short, well-focused, and supports Section 3.1.

---

# **Soft Spots (Important Nuances)**

## **1. “Unlike other T cell subsets, Tregs depend predominantly on FAO” needs tightening**
This is one of the *most frequently oversimplified* immunometabolism statements.

Correct nuance:  
• Tregs use **both glycolysis and FAO**.  
• FAO/OXPHOS dominates **maintenance and suppressive function**, not early activation.  
• Th17 rely more on glycolysis → mTOR → HIF-1α → RORγt.

So the sentence is *directionally correct*, but a reviewer in T-cell metabolism will immediately note the absolutism.

---

## **2. PPARγ is not the primary FAO regulator in Tregs**
This is the biggest biological overstatement.

Modern consensus:  
• **PPARδ** (PPARβ/δ) is the dominant FAO regulator in most T-cell populations.  
• AMPK–ACC–malonyl-CoA–CPT1 axis is the central biochemical mechanism.  
• PPARγ plays supportive roles: lipid uptake, anti-inflammatory transcription, skin-resident Treg maintenance, tissue adaptation.

Your cited paper (Immunity 2023) shows PPARγ is important in **skin-resident Tregs**, but not as a universal master switch.

This doesn’t invalidate your narrative—you just need softer framing.

---

## **3. “PPARγ activation promotes FAO, which in turn supports Treg lineage commitment”**
This is true **in some contexts**, but the causal chain is more complex.

Correct mechanistic logic:  
PPARγ → CD36 / FABP / lipid uptake → substrate availability  
PPARγ → anti-inflammatory transcription  
FAO → acetyl-CoA → Foxp3 stability → Treg differentiation  
AMPK/PPARδ → dominant FAO control  
CPT1A → mitochondrial import

PPARγ is mostly upstream of *lipid handling*, not mitochondrial β-oxidation machinery.

Again: refine causality, not reject it.

---

## **4. “PPARγ enhances Foxp3 expression… through FAO upregulation”**
This is accurate for *some* systems, but Foxp3 enhancement is:
• epigenetic (acetylation)  
• mitochondrial-fitness-dependent  
• redox-regulated  
• driven by acetyl-CoA availability  

PPARγ itself does not directly control Foxp3.  
FAO → acetyl-CoA → histone acetylation does.

So the text should reflect that PPARγ indirectly shapes the metabolic landscape.

---

## **5. Missing one link: why PPARγ matters in *psoriasis***  
You mention therapeutic potential but do not anchor PPARγ’s role in:
• skin-resident Tregs  
• psoriasis lesions  
• keratinocyte signaling  
• inflammation control  

One sentence would solidify the clinical relevance.

---

# **What Works Beautifully**
• The conceptual spine is intact  
• You correctly cite PPARγ agonists in immune tolerance  
• The writing is clear and uncluttered  
• Nicely ties to sections 3.1 and 2  
• Sets up the SCFA section well (metabolic programming)

---

# **Verdict (Loose, Professorial)**
This subsection is **good but oversimplified**.

The biology is essentially right, but you need to:  
• soften PPARγ absolutism  
• add nuance discussing PPARδ/AMPK roles  
• avoid implying PPARγ directly induces CPT1A or Foxp3  
• maintain the causal chain but acknowledge complexity

Once those adjustments are made, this will read like a high-level, credible immunometabolism analysis.

### 35. **Simos:**

3.3 FAO Suppresses Glycolysis and Th17 Cell Differentiation

While FAO primarily fuels Treg cells, glycolysis predominantly supports Th17 cells. FAO enhances Foxp3 expression and promotes the secretion of IL-10 and TGF-β in Treg cells [20], whereas glycolysis facilitates RorγT expression and boosts IL-17 and IL-23 production in Th17 cells [35]. 

These observations suggest that FAO can counterbalance glycolysis, thereby suppressing Th17 cell differentiation and pro-inflammatory cytokine production. In vitro, Treg cells exhibit a higher rate of lipid oxidation and lower glycolytic flux than effector CD4⁺ T cells, indicating that increased FAO suppresses Th17 differentiation and promotes Treg expansion [36]. 

Glycolysis-derived lactate promotes RorγT transcription, whereas FAO-derived
metabolites favor Treg transcription [33]. In psoriasis, enhanced glycolysis promotes pathological skin changes and lactate accumulation, which augments IL-17 production by γδT cells. In murine models, genetic ablation of Rorγt or pharmacological inhibition of lactate-generating enzymes or transporters reduces skin pathology and IL-17A expression [37]. 

These findings suggest that boosting FAO while suppressing glycolysis downregulates Th17 transcription and inflammatory cytokine production, ultimately ameliorating psoriasis-like inflammation. AMPK plays a critical role in promoting FAO and inhibiting glycolysis. AMPK induces CPT1 expression and thereby increases FAO [38]. 

In contrast, mTOR signaling suppresses CPT1 and FAO while enhancing aerobic glycolysis [39], suggesting a mutual antagonism between AMPK and mTOR. During CD4⁺ T cell activation, blockade of mTOR signaling enhances FAO, promotes Treg development, and reduces glycolysis [40].

In psoriatic arthritis models, AMPK also mitigates disease
In review progression by inhibiting Th17 differentiation [41]. 

Therefore, AMPK orchestrates metabolic reprogramming through FAO promotion and glycolysis inhibition, restoring the Treg/Th17 axis and reducing psoriatic inflammation.

---

• AMPK → FAO ↑, glycolysis ↓  
• mTOR → glycolysis ↑, FAO ↓

This is textbook immunometabolic fate control.

# **Soft Spots / Nuances to Fix (Gentle, Professor Tone)**

## **1. “FAO suppresses glycolysis” is conceptually correct but needs nuance**
FAO does *not directly block* glycolysis.  
It *indirectly* suppresses glycolysis by:

• supporting ATP levels → less AMPK activation required  
• lowering HIF-1α expression  
• generating acetyl-CoA affecting chromatin  
• altering NADH/NAD+ ratio  
• reducing mTOR signaling via upstream nutrient sensing

Your statement is fine, but a reviewer will catch causal over-simplification.

---

**Acetyl-CoA → H3K27 acetylation → Foxp3 locus opening.**

## **3. “AMPK induces CPT1 expression” is not technically correct**
This is similar to Section 3.1.

AMPK **increases CPT1 activity indirectly** by inhibiting ACC1/ACC2 → lowering malonyl-CoA, which inhibits CPT1.

AMPK does *not* directly induce transcription of CPT1A.

Easy fix later, but it’s a core mechanistic correction.

## **4. “mTOR suppresses CPT1” is correct but the mechanism is the reverse: mTOR promotes glycolysis**
The CPT1 effect is:

mTOR → increased glycolysis → decreased reliance on FAO → reduced CPT1A-driven β-oxidation  
(not direct repression of CPT1 transcription)

Again, minor but worth refining.

## **5. Lactate → γδT cell IL-17 production is excellent but could use a reference tie-in**
You did cite [37] later—good.  
This is a very strong psoriasis-specific mechanistic point and worth keeping.

**HIF-1α directly binds and promotes RORγt while targeting Foxp3 for degradation.**

One sentence about this would elevate the immunological completeness.

This is a classic point in metabolic Th17/Treg differentiation.

This section is more balanced, but upstream interpretations will resonate here.

### 37. **Simos:**

3.4 PPARγ Inhibits Th17 Differentiation by Enhancing FAO

PPARγ alleviates psoriatic inflammation primarily through metabolic reprogramming—enhancing FAO and repressing glycolysis—to restore the Treg/Th17 axis. Kim et al. [42] reported that PPARγ agonists inhibit Th17 differentiation and reduce IL-17 and IL-22 production by promoting FAO. PPARγ also suppresses Th17 transcription by downregulating RorγT via FAO enhancement [43]. 

Conversely, PPARγ-deficient mice exhibit significantly elevated IL-17A and IL-17F mRNA levels in CD4⁺ T lymphocytes compared to wild-type controls [44]. 

Similarly, in colonic tissues from inflammatory bowel disease mouse models, PPARγ deficiency in macrophages leads to upregulation of IL-22 [45]. 

These studies collectively indicate that PPARγ activation downregulates Th17-associated cytokines IL-17 and IL-22, whereas PPARγ deficiency enhances their expression. Since these cytokines are well-established drivers of psoriatic inflammation, PPARγ-mediated FAO enhancement plays crucial role in reducing Th17 activity and its associated inflammatory cytokine profile (Figure 1).



---

# **Overall Impression**
This section is **coherent, readable, and consistent with the narrative arc** you established earlier.  
But it also represents the **highest-risk section** so far in terms of mechanistic overreach.

Why?

Because **PPARγ’s effect on Th17 cells is far less direct and far less well-established** than its effects on Tregs, lipid handling, tissue-resident immune tolerance, or adipose biology.

So while the text is *not wrong*, it is more fragile than earlier sections.

Still, the section flows nicely and is easy to follow.

# **Strengths**

PPARγ activation → FAO ↑ → glycolysis ↓ → Th17 ↓ → IL-17/IL-22 ↓ → psoriasis improvement.

This narrative is persuasive and matches the metabolic immune logic.

• PPARγ agonists reduce Th17 differentiation  
• PPARγ deficiency increases IL-17 family cytokines  
• PPARγ deficiency increases IL-22 in tissue macrophages  

—these are good citations.

### **3. Excellent clinical relevance**
IL-17 and IL-22 are core psoriasis cytokines.  
Linking PPARγ to suppression of these is highly relevant for dermatology.

### **4. Well-integrated with metabolism**
You correctly frame the mechanism through FAO upregulation and glycolysis repression.

### **5. Strong narrative flow**
This subsection transitions neatly from 3.3 and sets up the broader therapeutic frame.

---

# **Soft Spots / Mechanistic Nuances (Professor Tone, Gentle but Important)**

## **1. PPARγ → FAO → Th17 suppression is not strongly established**
This is the main intellectual issue.

What is well-established:  
• Glycolysis promotes Th17 differentiation (via HIF-1α, mTOR, RORγt).  
• FAO/OXPHOS promotes Treg differentiation.

What is *less* established:  
**PPARγ directly suppresses Th17 via FAO.**

The literature is thinner here and mostly indirect:
• PPARγ agonists → increased Treg → reduced Th17  
• PPARγ anti-inflammatory actions → decreased cytokines  
• PPARγ-deficient animals → more inflammation / IL-17

You must soften causality to avoid reviewer pushback.

---

## **2. “PPARγ downregulates RORγt via FAO enhancement” is an oversimplification**
RORγt is regulated by:
• HIF-1α  
• mTORC1  
• glycolytic flux  
• amino acid sensors  
• nuclear receptors (RAR, AHR)

PPARγ is not known to *directly* repress RORγt transcription.  
Any effect is likely:

**PPARγ → FAO shift → reduced HIF-1α/mTOR → reduced RORγt**

So this must be phrased as *indirect.*

---

## **3. Macrophage IL-22 vs T cell IL-22**
You mention IBD models where macrophage-specific PPARγ deficiency → IL-22 ↑.

This is scientifically valid, but IL-22 in psoriasis comes mostly from:
• Th17  
• Th22  
• γδ T cells  
• ILC3

So using macrophage IL-22 as evidence for *Th17* regulation is a stretch.  
A reviewer will flag this unless you clarify:

“…although IL-22 in psoriasis is mainly produced by Th17/Th22 cells, macrophage PPARγ deficiency also increases IL-22 in colitis models, underscoring a broader anti-inflammatory role.”

That protects the argument.

---

## **4. Figure 1 reference is fine, but ensure the figure actually illustrates FAO → Th17 suppression**
Reviewers dislike mismatched figures.

---

## **5. The section needs one line acknowledging PPARδ and AMPK**
Because saying “PPARγ inhibits Th17 by enhancing FAO” implies PPARγ is the primary FAO regulator.  
But earlier sections already established nuance—repeat it here:

“…PPARγ contributes to FAO-enhanced Treg–Th17 balance, although PPARδ and AMPK–CPT1A remain the primary drivers of β-oxidation in T cells.”

This single sentence immunizes the whole subsection.

---

• You tie cytokine outputs (IL-17, IL-22) directly to psoriasis.  
• The writing is crisp and efficient.  
• The subsection doesn’t overstay its welcome.  
• The logic is consistent with the broader narrative.

This section is **conceptually correct**, but **overstates PPARγ’s direct role** in Th17 inhibition and needs softening for mechanistic accuracy.

Once the causal arrows are calibrated, this will be a clean, credible subsection that passes immunometabolism review easily.

### 39. **Simos:**

4.1 Cellular Entry Pathways of SCFAs

SCFAs enter cells via passive diffusion, carrier-mediated transport, and receptor activation, each modulating immune responses. Passive diffusion allows SCFAs to cross intestinal epithelia into circulation. Carrier proteins include sodium-coupled monocarboxylate transporter 1 (SMCT1/SLC5A8), which co-transports SCFAs (e.g., propionate, butyrate) with Na⁺, influencing indoleamine 2,3-dioxygenase 1 (IDO1) and aldehyde dehydrogenase 1A2 (ALDH1A2) in dendritic cells (DCs), promoting regulatory T cell (Treg) differentiation [46]. 

Monocarboxylate transporter 1 (MCT1/SLC16A1) facilitates pH-dependent SCFA
transport (e.g., acetate, propionate) without Na⁺ coupling [47]. 

Additionally, SCFAs activate receptors like GPR41, GPR43, GPR109A, Olfr78, OR51E2, aryl hydrocarbon receptor (AHR), and peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor γ (PPARγ) [48]. 

Receptor binding triggers signaling cascades regulating inflammation and immunity. Recent studies show SCFAs modulate cytokine production in neutrophils, macrophages, DCs, and T cells, aiding inflammation resolution [49]. 

These mechanisms highlight SCFAs' immunomodulatory potential and therapeutic relevance in psoriasis.


---

AHR is activated by tryptophan metabolites (indoles), flavonoids, pollutants—  
not by SCFAs in a classical ligand-binding sense.

SCFAs may **modulate** AHR indirectly, but they are not canonical AHR ligands.

This is a subtle but real mechanistic nuance.

You don’t need to remove AHR—just phrase carefully later.

SCFA diffusion occurs mainly in their **protonated (undissociated)** form.  
You may want to clarify this eventually.

### 41. **Simos:**

4.2 Role of SCFAs in T Cell-Mediated Psoriasis Modulation

The pathogenesis of psoriasis is largely driven by the TNF-α/IL-23/IL-17 axis and aberrant proliferation and differentiation of epidermal keratinocytes [50]. 

IL-23 promotes the differentiation of Tregs into T helper 17 (Th17) cells, while IL-17A suppresses TGF-β1 production and Foxp3 expression, thereby inhibiting Treg activity [51]. 

The balance between Th17 and Treg cells is critical in psoriasis. Tregs exert
immunosuppressive functions through direct cell–cell contact, secretion of inhibitory cytokines (e.g., IL-10, TGF-β, IL-35), and cytotoxic mechanisms targeting effector T cells [52]. 

SCFAs can enhance the proliferation of thymus-derived Tregs (tTregs) via surface receptor interaction. Notably, butyrate binds to GPR41 expressed on medullary thymic epithelial cells, inducing the expression of autoimmune regulator (AIRE), thereby promoting tTreg development [53]. Intracellularly, SCFAs regulate T cell differentiation
(Th17/Treg) and apoptosis, as well as the release of cytokines such as IL-6 and IL-10. Upon cellular entry, SCFAs inhibit histone deacetylase (HDAC) activity, thereby altering gene expression profiles [54].

Specifically, SCFAs act as HDAC inhibitors, promoting H3 histone acetylation at the Foxp3 gene locus, which drives the expression of Foxp3 in naive CD4⁺ T cells and their subsequent differentiation into peripheral Tregs (pTregs) [55]. 

Under certain conditions, SCFAs suppress the production of pro-inflammatory cytokines such as IL-6 and IL-23, which are involved in the activation of the RAR-related orphan receptor γt (RORγt) and Th17 cell differentiation. By dampening the expression of these cytokines, SCFAs reduce Th17 cell frequency [56]. 

Thus, modulating the Th17/Treg cell ratio via SCFAs presents a promising therapeutic avenue in psoriasis (Table1).

---

Instead:

• IL-23 expands precursors committed to Th17-like fates  
• IL-23 stabilizes the Th17 phenotype  
• IL-23 suppresses Treg stability under certain inflammatory conditions  
• There is some *plasticity*, but not direct conversion

A reviewer will catch this wording.

### 43. **Simos:**

4.3 SCFAs in Neutrophil-Mediated Modulation of Psoriasis

Neutrophilic infiltration is a hallmark of psoriatic skin, contributing to the formation of neutrophil extracellular traps (NETs) [57]. 

In psoriasis, neutrophils release enzymes such as myeloperoxidase (MPO),
neutrophil elastase (NE), proteinase 3, and cathepsin G, which generate reactive oxygen species (ROS), activate inflammatory mediators, and produce autoantigens [58]. 

Neutrophils also express numerous receptors, including Toll-like receptors (TLRs) and NOD-like receptors (NLRs), which promote the production of inflammatory cytokines (e.g., TNF-α, IL-1, IL-4, IL-6) and chemokines (e.g., CXCL1, CCL2), further aggravating psoriatic inflammation [59]. 

Neutrophils in psoriatic patients exacerbate skin inflammation via NET-mediated activation of IL-36 and TLR4 signaling pathways [60]. 

Recent findings suggest that neutrophilia characterizes generalized pustular psoriasis, and depletion of neutrophils alleviates symptoms in refractory cases [61]. 

Experimental elevation of SCFA concentrations in murine models significantly reduced psoriatic lesion formation, epidermal proliferation (Ki67⁺ cells), and the expression of pro-inflammatory genes including IL-17A, IL-17F, IL-17C, IL-22, IL-1β, IL-6, TNF-α, CXCL1, and CCL20.

Concurrently, SCFAs upregulated TGF-β1 and IL-10 expression [62]. 

These findings suggest that SCFAs may limit neutrophil infiltration and reduce inflammatory mediator release, thereby attenuating psoriasis progression.

### 45. **Simos:**


4.4 SCFAs in Dendritic Cell-Mediated Modulation of Psoriasis

The skin harbors a complex network of dendritic cells (DCs), including Langerhans cells (LCs), conventional dermal DCs (cDCs), plasmacytoid DCs (pDCs), and inflammatory DCs (iDCs). As primary producers of type I interferons (e.g., IFN-α), TNF-α, IL-12, and IL-23, DCs are instrumental in psoriatic pathogenesis [63]. 

pDCs recognize viral nucleic acids and, via TLR7 and TLR9 activation, produce large
quantities of IFN-α [64].

 IFN-α serves as an upstream trigger of the IL-23/IL-17 axis by inducing immature cDCs to secrete IL-12, IL-15, IL-18, and IL-23 [65]. Additionally, antimicrobial peptides such as human β-defensins (hBD2, hBD3) and lysozyme can aggregate self-DNA or RNA into microparticles, which are phagocytosed by pDCs and activate TLR7, TLR8, and TLR9, contributing to psoriatic inflammation [66].

SCFAs have been shown to attenuate the antigen-presenting capabilities of DCs, thereby reducing the activation of autoreactive T cells [67]. 

SCFAs, particularly butyrate, downregulate lipopolysaccharide (LPS)-induced expression of DC activation markers and pro-inflammatory cytokine release [68]. 

Siemann et al. demonstrated that n-butyrate suppresses homotypic DC aggregation, inhibits IL-12 production, maintains IL-10 secretion, and blocks nuclear translocation of NF-κB at the molecular level [69]. 

These findings suggest that targeting DCs through SCFA-mediated modulation warrants further investigation in the context of psoriasis therapy.

---

### 47. **Simos:**

4.5 SCFAs in Macrophage-Mediated Modulation of Psoriasis

Macrophages are pivotal regulators of immune responses and are heavily involved in psoriatic pathology.

Two macrophage phenotypes exist: pro-inflammatory M1 and anti-inflammatory M2 subtypes. Psoriatic lesions are characterized by increased M1 and decreased M2 macrophage populations [70].

These macrophages produce inflammatory mediators such as TNF-α. M1 macrophages also express inducible nitric oxide synthase (iNOS), IL-23p19, and IL-12/23p40, all of which are central to psoriasis pathogenesis [71]. 

SCFAs have been implicated in modulating macrophage polarization. Specifically, SCFAs promote M1-to-M2 phenotype switching and suppress the production of inflammatory mediators such as TNF-α [72].

Furthermore, SCFAs—especially butyrate—can inhibit the NOD-like receptor family pyrin domain containing 3 (NLRP3) inflammasome pathway, thereby preventing macrophage activation and reducing the secretion of pro-inflammatory cytokines IL-1β and IL-18 [73]. 
These data support the notion that macrophage polarization is a key mechanism underlying the anti-inflammatory effects of SCFAs in
psoriasis.

---

This keeps the intuitive, high-level, constructive tone—pointing out strengths, biological accuracy, and areas needing nuance.

Not strict reviewer mode yet.

### 49. **Simos:**

5. Advances in SCFA-Based Therapeutic Strategies for Psoriasis

Topical administration of SCFAs has been shown to promote the growth of beneficial microbiota, while the direct application of probiotics to the skin can in turn stimulate SCFA release, thereby alleviating cutaneous inflammation.

A study investigating the effects of butyrate treatment in psoriasis demonstrated that topical
application of butyrate significantly reduced skin inflammation and the expression of psoriasis-associated inflammatory mediators compared to controls [74]. 

However, it is noteworthy that in imiquimod-induced psoriasis-like animal models, topical or systemic administration of acetate exacerbated psoriatic features
[75]. 

These findings suggest that the effects of SCFAs are context-dependent, exhibiting both
pro-inflammatory and anti-inflammatory properties under different environmental conditions.

A major limitation in the clinical application of SCFAs is their unpleasant odor and their requirement for relatively high concentrations to exert therapeutic effects. To overcome these challenges, researchers have developed SCFA derivatives, such as BA-NH-NH-BA, which comprises two butyrate molecules linked via an -NH-O-NH- connector. 

Compared to native butyrate, this derivative exhibits reduced histone deacetylase (HDAC) inhibitory activity, while significantly attenuating Staphylococcus aureus proliferation and the
associated inflammatory responses in murine models [76].

The beneficial impact of the gut microbiota on host health is closely associated with SCFA production, and oral supplementation with SCFAs may exert anti-inflammatory effects on the skin. 

Rather et al. [77] demonstrated that ethanol extracts from the probiotic Lactobacillus sakei proBio-65 had marked therapeutic effects on imiquimod-induced psoriasis-like skin inflammation in mice. Regular consumption of cheese may introduce probiotics and enhance SCFA production. 

Yoshida et al. [62] further showed in animal studies that dietary supplementation with inulin, a fermentable fiber, ameliorated imiquimod-induced psoriasis-like dermatitis in mice, with the protective effects of dietary fiber potentially mediated through microbiota-driven SCFA synthesis.


---

# **Soft Spots / Required Nuances (Gentle, Professor Tone)**

## **1. “Topical application of probiotics stimulates SCFA release” is oversimplified**
On skin, **lactobacilli do not reliably produce SCFAs**.  
Skin microbiota favor:

• lactate  
• succinate  
• some short-chain amino acid derivatives  

Classic SCFA production (acetate, propionate, butyrate) is mostly **gut microbial**, not skin microbial.

You can keep it by softening:

“Some topical probiotics modulate metabolite profiles—including low-level SCFAs—which may contribute to anti-inflammatory effects.”

Otherwise reviewers might challenge this.

---

## **2. The acetate paradox needs mechanistic context**
Acetate *worsening* IMQ-induced inflammation is well known, but the WHY matters.

Acetate can:

• fuel inflammatory glycolysis  
• activate GPR43 broadly  
• enhance IL-1β release under some contexts  
• increase keratinocyte proliferation  

Without mechanistic clarification, it looks contradictory.

A line like:  
“Acetate’s pro-inflammatory effects may depend on dose, tissue environment, and receptor expression patterns.”

—would help.

---

## **3. SCFA derivatives section needs clearer purpose**
You say BA-NH-NH-BA has:

• reduced HDAC inhibition  
• antimicrobial effects  
• reduced inflammation  

But a reviewer will ask:

“Why use a derivative with **less** HDAC inhibition?”

Answer:  
to reduce odor, improve stability, and target specific pathways.

Worth clarifying.

---

## **4. The cheese sentence is scientifically weak**
Regular cheese consumption → probiotic delivery → SCFA production  

This line is **too casual** for a high-impact scientific review.

Cheese microbiota **rarely** produce SCFAs in meaningful quantities in the gut.

Replace with:  
“Fermented foods can introduce probiotic organisms that may indirectly influence gut metabolite profiles, including SCFAs.”

This maintains safety.

---

## **5. Need to clarify that L. sakei effects may be *SCFA-independent***
Rather et al.’s study likely involved:

• immune-modulatory metabolites  
• cell wall components  
• lactic acid  
• anti-inflammatory peptides  

Not necessarily SCFAs.

You should phrase it more carefully:

“…which may act partially through SCFA-related mechanisms, though other immunomodulatory metabolites are also likely involved.”

---

## **6. Inulin → SCFA → psoriasis improvement needs mechanistic anchoring**
You mention the study correctly, but a reviewer will want a biochemical rationale:

• inulin → fermentation → butyrate/propionate ↑  
• SCFAs → Treg enhancement  
• Th17 suppression  
• IL-17 and IL-23 modulation  
• reduced keratinocyte hyperproliferation  

You can add this later.

### 51. **Simos:**

6. Conclusion

The pathogenesis of psoriasis is intricately linked to metabolic and immune dysregulation, particularly the
imbalance between pro-inflammatory Th17 cells and immunosuppressive Tregs. PPARγ emerges as a
pivotal mediator of this equilibrium by enhancing FAO—a metabolic pathway essential for Treg
differentiation and function—while concurrently suppressing glycolysis-dependent Th17 responses.
Pharmacological activation of PPARγ or its downstream targets represents a promising therapeutic avenue
to restore immune homeostasis. Complementarily, SCFAs, derived from gut microbiota, modulate psoriasis
through dual mechanisms: epigenetic regulation and receptor-mediated signaling. Preclinical studies
underscore the efficacy of SCFA derivatives, topical probiotics, and dietary fiber in mitigating psoriatic
inflammation, though clinical translation requires optimization of delivery and dosing. Future research
should explore synergistic strategies combining PPARγ agonists with SCFA-based therapies to amplify
metabolic reprogramming and microbiome-immune crosstalk. Together, these advances illuminate a
paradigm shift toward precision medicine in psoriasis, targeting metabolic-immune networks for sustained
remission.

---

### 56. **ChatGPT:**

# **Narrative Expert Review (Flowing with the Manuscript)**

## **Abstract**
The abstract captures the central thesis elegantly: a dual framework where **PPARγ-driven FAO** and **SCFA-mediated immunomodulation** converge to rebalance the Treg/Th17 axis. The mechanistic summaries are accurate and the therapeutic framing is clear.

However, the abstract compresses a *very* complex biological landscape into a structure that risks oversimplifying the distinct roles of PPARγ, PPARδ, AMPK, and SCFA receptors. The text gives the impression that PPARγ is the singular conductor of FAO in Tregs, when in fact Treg FAO is a multi-nodal program with AMPK–ACC–CPT1 and PPARδ at its core. This is not a fatal issue at the abstract level, but a reviewer will expect this nuance to emerge later.  

The final sentence (“proposing novel metabolic and microbiome-targeted strategies…”) is good, though the abstract could mention the therapeutic complexity—namely that acetate can worsen disease under specific conditions. Mentioning context dependence would foreshadow your later nuance beautifully.

Still: it’s a strong abstract that reads well.

---

## **Introduction**
The introduction is clear, correct, and conventional. You accurately integrate the immunology of psoriasis with the metabolic underpinnings of Treg and Th17 lineages.

Two observations:

1. **You handle the Th17/Treg dichotomy cleanly**, with sound cytokine descriptions (IL-17, IL-22 vs IL-10, TGF-β).  
2. **You frame FAO vs glycolysis correctly**, though again PPARγ is presented as a more dominant FAO governor than current literature supports.

The introduction is functional but not yet distinctive. It does its job, but it could benefit from briefly acknowledging that psoriasis is increasingly seen as a **metabolic-inflammatory disease**—a “metaflammatory” state—where lipid metabolism, mitochondrial signaling, and nutrient sensing deeply shape cutaneous immunity.

Nonetheless, the framing is solid and sets a good tone.

---

## **Section 2 — PPARγ in FAO: Mechanistic Ambition with Slight Overreach**
This is one of your strongest technical sections, rich in detail and confident in its mechanistic voice. You clearly understand FAO, CD36, FATPs, CPT1, PGC-1α, AMPK, and mitochondrial regulation.

But the section suffers from **PPARγ absolutism**—a persistent tendency to present PPARγ as the central, unified driver of Treg FAO.  

A modern immunometabolomics reviewer will know that:

- **PPARδ (β/δ)** is the dominant FAO regulator in T cells.  
- **AMPK–ACC–malonyl-CoA–CPT1A** is the biochemical heart of FAO activation.  
- **PPARγ’s role is context-dependent**, strongest in tissue-resident Tregs and lipid-rich environments (skin, adipose).  

Your references do support many of your statements, but they come from mixed contexts (skin, adipose, cancer, macrophages) that require careful phrasing to avoid overstating universality.

Mechanistically, the material is excellent; the framing just needs calibration.  
You narrate FAO beautifully—better than many published reviews—but you need to soften the implication that PPARγ single-handedly orchestrates the entire FAO program.

Still, this section is deeply knowledgeable and a pleasure to read.

---

## **Section 3.1 — FAO Fuels Tregs: Strong but Missing Epigenetic Gold**
This is one of the best-written mechanistic passages in the manuscript. The logic is crisp:

FAO → acetyl-CoA → Foxp3 stability → Treg maintenance  
Glycolysis → mTOR → RORγt → Th17 phenotype  

You correctly cite CD36, AMPK, CPT1A, and SCFA-mediated acetylation (later), and the flow is strong. But two refinements are needed:

1. **Tregs do not rely exclusively on FAO**. They use both FAO and glycolysis depending on activation state.  
2. **The real mechanistic jewel—histone acetylation via acetyl-CoA—should be given more prominence.**  

A reviewer will want to see the chromatin mechanism emphasized, because it is the single cleanest explanation for FAO’s lineage effects.

Nonetheless: excellent biochemical clarity, near publication-ready.

---

## **Section 3.2 — PPARγ and Treg Differentiation**
A compact, effective subsection.  
It reiterates PPARγ’s influence on FAO and Foxp3 expression, but again leans too heavily on PPARγ as the central FAO conductor.

This is not incorrect—but the literature would frame PPARγ’s action as one node in a broader metabolic decision tree. You could strengthen scientific honesty by acknowledging AMPK and PPARδ directly here.

---

## **Section 3.3 — FAO Suppresses Glycolysis and Th17 Differentiation**
This section is superb.  
You clearly explain the metabolic antagonism between FAO-driven Treg stability and glycolysis-driven Th17 differentiation.

You also incorporate lactate biology, γδ T cells, and RORγt suppression—excellent, psoriasis-specific mechanistic insight.

The only correction needed is biochemical:

- AMPK does **not** phosphorylate CPT1A directly; it inhibits ACC1/2, lowering malonyl-CoA.  
- mTOR does not directly “suppress CPT1” but shifts metabolism toward glycolysis.  

These are simple but important mechanical nuances that strengthen credibility.

Otherwise: outstanding.

---

## **Section 3.4 — PPARγ Inhibits Th17 Cells**
This is your most fragile section.  
The evidence for PPARγ’s direct suppression of Th17 differentiation is thinner than for Tregs, and the effects are often indirect through metabolic context or Treg expansion.

You correctly cite PPARγ-deficient animals showing elevated IL-17/IL-22, but the causal chain runs:

PPARγ → anti-inflammatory transcription + lipid handling  
FAO → epigenetic + metabolic shifts  
↓  
reduced HIF-1α / mTOR  
↓  
reduced RORγt

This is indirect, and needs to be framed as such.

The macrophage IL-22 example is valid but not directly about Th17 cells; a reviewer will want you to note that IL-22 in psoriasis predominantly comes from Th17/Th22 cells and ILC3, not macrophages.

Still, the section is coherent—it just needs softer causal arrows.

---
