# **Frontiers Reviewer Report**

**Manuscript Title:** *PPARγ-Driven Fatty Acid Oxidation and Short-Chain Fatty Acids as Metabolic Regulators of Psoriasis*

**General Assessment**  
This manuscript provides an ambitious and intellectually rich synthesis on the intersection of metabolic programming, immune cell differentiation, and psoriatic inflammation. The authors successfully integrate two rapidly advancing domains—PPARγ-mediated fatty acid oxidation (FAO) and short-chain fatty acid (SCFA) immunomodulation—and place them within the context of Th17/Treg imbalance, keratinocyte hyperproliferation, and innate immune activation.  

The review is well structured, clearly written, and conceptually coherent. Its strengths include sophisticated handling of T-cell metabolism, appropriate use of FAO–epigenetic links, and a detailed discussion of SCFA transporters, receptors, and intracellular signaling. The sections on neutrophils, dendritic cells, and macrophages stand out as particularly clear and clinically relevant.  

However, several mechanistic claims overstate the roles of PPARγ and SCFAs, and a number of biochemical pathways require more nuance to reflect current consensus. These concerns do not undermine the overall contribution but must be addressed for scientific precision.

With revisions, this manuscript will constitute a high-quality and timely review suitable for publication in a leading Frontiers immunology or dermatology journal.

---

# **Major Comments**

### **1. Overstatement of PPARγ as the Primary Regulator of FAO in T Cells**
Across sections 2–3.4, the manuscript repeatedly frames PPARγ as the central orchestrator of FAO and Treg differentiation. While PPARγ is important—particularly in tissue-resident Tregs in skin and adipose—FAO in T cells is primarily driven by:

- **PPARδ (PPARβ/δ)**  
- **AMPK–ACC–malonyl-CoA–CPT1A axis**  
- **PGC-1α-dependent mitochondrial programs**

PPARγ contributes, but does not singularly direct FAO.  
The manuscript should temper causal statements, clarify PPARγ’s context-specific role, and acknowledge the dominant AMPK/PPARδ pathways.

This revision is essential for mechanistic accuracy.

---

### **2. Several Metabolic Mechanisms Require Correction**
Two biochemical inaccuracies appear consistently:

**a. AMPK does not phosphorylate CPT1A directly.**  
Its effect is mediated through inhibition of ACC1/2 → reduced malonyl-CoA → relieved CPT1A inhibition.  
All text describing AMPK → CPT1A activation should be revised accordingly.

**b. mTOR does not directly suppress CPT1A.**  
Rather, mTOR promotes glycolysis and anabolic metabolism, indirectly disfavoring FAO.  
This distinction should be reflected in Sections 3.3 and 3.4.

These are important core corrections.

---

### **3. IL-23 Does Not Convert Tregs into Th17 Cells**
In Section 4.2, the claim that IL-23 “promotes the differentiation of Tregs into Th17 cells” is inaccurate. IL-23 expands Th17 cells and destabilizes Treg function, but does not induce direct Treg→Th17 transdifferentiation.

Please rephrase to reflect modern immunology literature.

---

### **4. Overgeneralization of SCFA Effects and Receptor Biology**
Some statements require refinement:

- SCFAs do **not** directly activate AHR; effects are indirect and context-dependent.  
- Olfr78/OR51E2 are not psoriasis-relevant receptors and should be minimized or contextualized.  
- Topical probiotics rarely generate appreciable SCFA levels on the skin; claims should be softened toward "metabolite modulation" rather than specific SCFA release.  
- Cheese contains probiotics but does not reliably enhance gut SCFA production; wording should reflect this.

These adjustments will improve rigor and avoid overinterpretation.

---

### **5. Acetate Paradox Requires Mechanistic Clarification**
You appropriately cite that acetate can worsen IMQ-induced psoriatic inflammation.  
However, the manuscript should provide a mechanistic rationale (GPR43 overactivation, enhanced glycolysis, keratinocyte proliferation) and emphasize that SCFA effects are **dose- and context-dependent**.

This is a valuable scientific nuance and strengthens the therapeutic discussion.

---

### **6. Missing Acknowledgment of IMQ Model Limitations**
Because multiple therapeutic conclusions are based on IMQ-induced dermatitis, the manuscript should acknowledge:

- IMQ activates TLR7/8 strongly  
- It does not recapitulate chronic plaque psoriasis  
- Neutrophil and γδT-cell dependence differs from human disease

This short addition increases scientific humility and strengthens credibility.

---

# **Minor Comments**

### **1. Enhance Epigenetic Explanations**
The acetyl-CoA → H3K27 acetylation → Foxp3 stabilization pathway is central to FAO-driven Treg fate and deserves consistent emphasis across Sections 3.1–3.3.

### **2. Clarify Innate Immune Cell Receptors**
Add brief notes on:

- GPR43/GPR109A expression on neutrophils and DCs  
- PPARγ’s known role in M2 macrophage polarization  

This connects Sections 2–4 more coherently.

### **3. Remove IL-4 from the list of neutrophil-associated cytokines**
IL-4 is not a psoriasis-relevant cytokine.

### **4. Improve Therapeutic Section Framing**
Clearly differentiate:

- SCFA derivatives  
- topical SCFAs  
- oral SCFA supplementation  
- dietary fiber → microbiota → SCFA pathway  

Some outcomes are SCFA-independent and should be noted.

### **5. Tone Down Certainty Around Synergistic PPARγ + SCFA Therapy**
This is an interesting idea, but purely hypothetical.  
Reframe as a promising research direction rather than a predicted clinical outcome.

---

# **Summary Recommendation: Minor Revisions**
The manuscript is well organized, deeply informed, and timely.  
It will make a valuable contribution once its mechanistic framing is sharpened and a few overstatements corrected.

The revisions required are largely conceptual and do not fundamentally alter the narrative.  
Once addressed, the article will meet the standards of a top-tier Frontiers review in immunometabolism and dermatology.

# **Frontiers Reviewer Report – Major Issues Only (Direct, High-Level, Non-Nuanced)**

## **General Assessment**
The manuscript is ambitious and covers a large mechanistic landscape.  
However, at a high-level, **three major structural problems** undermine its scientific precision:

1. **PPARγ is overstated as the master regulator of FAO.**  
2. **SCFA biology is overgeneralized and sometimes biologically implausible in the context of skin.**  
3. **The therapeutic section mixes mechanistic evidence with speculative or weakly supported claims.**

These are *major* issues that must be corrected for the review to be scientifically defensible.

---

# **Major Issue 1 — PPARγ Is Dramatically Overplayed**
Throughout the manuscript, PPARγ is presented as if:

- it governs FAO broadly across immune cells  
- it drives Treg metabolism as the dominant factor  
- it directly suppresses Th17 glycolysis  
- it coordinates mitochondrial biogenesis  

None of these statements are universally supported.

**In reality:**

- **PPARδ**, not PPARγ, is the principal FAO regulator in T cells.  
- **AMPK–ACC–CPT1A**, not PPARγ, controls FAO flux.  
- PPARγ’s role is context-dependent (skin, adipose, tumor microenvironment).  

As written, the review gives an **incorrect hierarchy of metabolic regulation** and will be flagged immediately by metabolomics reviewers.

This must be corrected globally.

---

# **Major Issue 2 — SCFA Mechanisms Are Overstated and Overextended**
The manuscript treats SCFA effects as:

- consistently immunosuppressive  
- epigenetically powerful across all cell types  
- applicable equally to gut and skin  
- reliably produced by topical probiotics  
- clearly beneficial in dietary forms (e.g., cheese)  

These claims are too broad.

**Problems:**

- SCFAs are mostly gut-restricted; the skin does **not** produce meaningful butyrate or propionate.  
- Topical probiotics do **not** generate significant SCFA quantities.  
- Acetate has well-documented pro-inflammatory effects—this receives only partial acknowledgement.  
- Dietary claims (cheese, probiotics) are speculative and not appropriate for a high-tier mechanistic review.

In sum: **SCFA biology is being stretched beyond the data.**

This requires a structural rewrite of the SCFA sections.

---

# **Major Issue 3 — The Therapeutic Discussion Is Too Speculative**
The therapeutic section blends:

- preclinical rodent findings  
- topical SCFA experiments  
- probiotic extracts  
- dietary fiber  
- SCFA derivatives  
- human clinical relevance (implied)  

The result is **heterogeneous evidence presented as if it were clinically coherent**.

Problems:

- Rodent IMQ models do not reflect human psoriasis sufficiently for therapeutic claims.  
- SCFA derivatives show antimicrobial effects but reduced HDAC inhibition—this contradiction is not explained.  
- The link between diet → microbiota → SCFAs → psoriasis is presented too confidently.

This entire section needs to be tightened, with speculation clearly labeled as such.

---

# **Major Issue 4 — Missing a Clear Mechanistic Hierarchy**
The review attempts to integrate:

- FAO  
- glycolysis  
- AMPK  
- mTOR  
- PPARs  
- CD36  
- SCFA receptors  
- HDAC inhibition  
- innate cell activation  

—but currently lacks a clear, unified model.

A high-tier review must articulate:

1. **What are the primary drivers?**  
2. **What pathways are secondary modifiers?**  
3. **How do metabolic and immunologic axes converge?**

Right now, the manuscript presents mechanisms in isolation rather than as a structured hierarchy.

---

# **Major Issue 5 — Overreliance on Non-Psoriasis Contexts**
Many mechanistic citations come from:

- oncology  
- gut inflammation  
- metabolic disease  
- general immunology  

This is acceptable, but the manuscript often **imports conclusions from unrelated systems** into psoriasis without acknowledging limitations.

A reviewer will demand clearer justification whenever non-skin or non-psoriatic data are used.

---

# **Major Issue 6 — Causal Chains Are Too Linear**
The manuscript frequently writes as if the following are single-step cause–effect relationships:

- PPARγ → FAO → Treg  
- FAO → Foxp3 → psoriasis improvement  
- SCFAs → HDAC inhibition → Treg differentiation  

But in reality, psoriasis involves:

- multi-layered feedback loops  
- redundant regulatory pathways  
- keratinocyte-intrinsic metabolic changes  
- strong involvement of innate immunity independent of T cells  

The manuscript oversimplifies these causal chains into linear models.

A more sober framing is required.

---

# **Major Issue 7 — Lack of Critical Limitations**
A high-level review must explicitly mention critical caveats:

- IMQ model limitations  
- species differences (mouse vs human)  
- FAO dependence varies by Treg subtype  
- SCFA receptors differ in expression across tissues  
- context-dependent effects (dose, metabolic state, tissue oxygenation)  

These limitations are currently absent.

---

# **Summary of Required Major Revisions**
To meet high-tier Frontiers standards, the manuscript must:

1. **Rewrite the PPARγ narrative to reflect accurate metabolic hierarchy.**  
2. **Substantially temper the SCFA sections to avoid overreach.**  
3. **Restructure the therapeutic section to distinguish speculation from evidence.**  
4. **Present a clear, integrated model of psoriasis immunometabolism.**  
5. **Acknowledge cross-contextual data limitations.**  
6. **Avoid linear causal narratives and adopt a systems-immunology perspective.**

These constitute **major revisions**, though largely conceptual rather than structural.

### 66. **ChatGPT:**

**DCs = Dendritic Cells.**

They are one of the most important innate immune cell types in psoriasis.

### Quick, clean biological definition:
**Dendritic cells (DCs)** are **antigen-presenting cells** that bridge innate and adaptive immunity.  
They detect danger signals, produce cytokines (especially IL-23, IL-12, IFN-α), and activate T cells.

### In psoriasis, DCs are absolutely central:
• **Plasmacytoid DCs (pDCs)** release **IFN-α**, triggering the cascade that activates the IL-23 / IL-17 axis.  
• **Conventional DCs (cDCs)** produce **IL-12 and IL-23**, driving Th1 and Th17 expansion.  
• **Inflammatory DCs** sustain chronic plaque inflammation.  
• **Langerhans cells** are skin-resident sentinel DCs.

So when I talk about “DCs,” I mean all dendritic cell subsets relevant to psoriasis:

- pDCs  
- cDC1 / cDC2  
- inflammatory dermal DCs  
- Langerhans cells  

They collectively act as the **innate immune ignition system** in psoriatic skin—detecting danger, producing cytokines, and activating T cells.

If you want, I can give you the **4–6 sentence “review-perfect” description** for DCs in psoriasis to use in your manuscript.

**Abstract (your version)**  
*Psoriasis is a chronic inflammatory skin disorder driven by dysregulation of the Treg/Th17 axis, where enhanced Th17 activity promotes keratinocyte proliferation and inflammation, while impaired Treg function exacerbates immune dysregulation. Emerging evidence highlights peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor γ (PPARγ) as a key regulator of fatty acid oxidation (FAO), a metabolic pathway critical for Treg differentiation and function. PPARγ activation enhances FAO via upregulation of CD36, CPT1, and AMPK signaling, while suppressing glycolysis, thereby skewing the Treg/Th17 balance toward immune tolerance. Concurrently, short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs), microbial metabolites with immunomodulatory properties. ameliorate psoriatic inflammation by promoting Treg expansion, inhibiting Th17 polarization, and modulating innate immune cells (neutrophils, dendritic cells, and macrophages). SCFAs exert their effects through receptor-dependent signaling and epigenetic mechanisms (HDAC inhibition), while derivative compounds and probiotic interventions enhance therapeutic potential. This review synthesizes mechanistic insights into PPARγ-driven FAO and SCFA-mediated immunomodulation, proposing novel metabolic and microbiome-targeted strategies for psoriasis treatment.*

---

# **Now — let’s “reread” the abstract in light of the correct title and the structure issues.**

We read with two questions:
1. **Does this abstract fit the title?** (“Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk in Psoriasis”)  
2. **Does it represent the body of the manuscript honestly?**

---

# **1. Title vs Abstract: Mismatch**

Your title promises:

**“Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk"**  
= lipid metabolism interacting with DCs, macrophages, neutrophils, keratinocytes.

But the abstract focuses overwhelmingly on:

✓ Treg/Th17    
✓ PPARγ → FAO → Tregs  
✓ SCFAs → Tregs  

Innate immunity is mentioned **only once**, very briefly, as a side note in parentheses.

There is **no explicit description** of how fatty acids “crosstalk” with innate immune cells.

This mismatch is the root structural problem.

The abstract belongs to a paper titled:

**“Metabolic Modulation of the Treg/Th17 Axis by PPARγ and SCFAs in Psoriasis.”**

Not the paper you THINK you're writing.

# ⭐ **SECTION 2 — PPARγ in FAO (Evaluated Against Abstract + Intro)**

Here is the distilled message of Section 2:

> It maintains Treg differentiation and function.  
> It is mechanistically tied to psoriasis through FAO.  

Now let’s evaluate.

---

# ⭐ 1. **Does Section 2 logically follow from the Introduction?**  
**Yes — perfectly.**

The introduction set up:

- Psoriasis is driven by Treg/Th17 metabolic imbalance  
- Tregs rely on FAO  
- PPARγ is introduced as an FAO regulator  
- The review will focus on PPARγ + SCFAs  

Section 2 picks up that thread cleanly and dives into:

- PPARγ → FAO  
- FAO → Treg fitness  
- Molecular details (CD36, CPT1A, FATP1, PGC1α, AMPK)

This is exactly the kind of expansion the intro prepares the reader for.

### **Verdict:**  
**Coherent. Consistent. Smooth transition.**

---

# ⭐ 2. **Does Section 2 match the abstract?**  
**Yes — flawlessly.**

The abstract promised:

- PPARγ is a key FAO regulator  
- Upregulates CD36, CPT1, AMPK  
- Suppresses glycolysis  
- Supports Treg differentiation  

Section 2 delivers all these points in detail.

### **Verdict:**  
**Strong alignment. No mismatch.**

---

# ⭐ 3. **Is Section 2 internally coherent with the manuscript's true identity?**  
**Yes AND no.**

### ✔ It is coherent with the **PPARγ–Treg** theme  
Section 2 is deeply mechanistic and supports what Sections 3.1 and 3.2 will discuss.

### ✖ But it is **not coherent with SCFAs**,  
which the abstract later says are the *second half* of the paper.

In Section 2:

- PPARγ is treated as a **primary metabolic regulator**  
- FAO pathways are treated as *self-contained*  
- No link is made to microbial metabolites  
- No anticipation of SCFAs altering FAO  
- No sense of a “two-arm” integrated metabolic model

Thus, Section 2 builds a very detailed standalone machinery,  
but **does not set up the later SCFA content** at all.

This is a structural tension:

> **PPARγ and SCFAs are presented as parallel mechanisms rather than interconnected ones.**

---

# ⭐ 4. **Does Section 2 set up later sections?**  
It sets up **Section 3** perfectly.  
But it fails to set up **Section 4 and 5**.

Why?

Because Section 2 never asks the question:

> “Are there external, microbial, dietary, or systemic fatty acids that influence PPARγ-driven FAO?”

If Section 2 were integrated, it would include something like:

- SCFAs can activate PPARγ  
- Butyrate fuels FAO  
- Microbial metabolites influence lipid oxidation  
- FAO and epigenetics interact  

Without this “bridge,” Section 4 feels like a new topic instead of a continuation.

---

# ⭐ 5. **Scientific Strengths of Section 2**
This is one of the strongest technical sections in the manuscript:

- mechanistic clarity  
- FA transport (CD36, FATPs)  
- mitochondrial import (CPT1/2)  
- β-oxidation + PGC-1α interactions  
- AMPK effects  
- transcriptional impacts (Foxp3)  

It is academically deep and well-referenced.

---

# ⭐ 6. **Where Section 2 goes wrong structurally**  
Structural flaw:

### ❌ It exaggerates PPARγ's role  
The section implicitly presents:

**PPARγ = master regulator of Treg FAO**  
which is biologically inaccurate.

The immune world knows:

- **PPARδ** regulates FAO in T cells more strongly than PPARγ  
- **AMPK/ACC/malonyl-CoA** is the true FAO throttle  
- PPARγ is more important in **tissue-resident Tregs, adipose Tregs, macrophages**

This overemphasis creates a hierarchy mismatch that becomes visible later when SCFAs come in — because SCFAs **do not naturally map onto PPARγ-centric FAO**, they map onto:

- HDAC inhibition  
- GPCR activation  
- anti-inflammatory shifts  

So Section 2 over-centers PPARγ in a way that doesn’t harmonize with SCFAs.

---

# ⭐ 7. **Narrative Problem: Section 2 does not prepare the reader for a dual-axis review**
The abstract promises:

**“PPARγ-driven FAO *and* SCFAs as parallel mechanisms.”**

But Section 2 behaves as if this is a review **only about PPARγ**.

It builds a self-contained world using:

- CD36  
- FATPs  
- CPT1  
- PGC1α  
- AMPK  
- FAO  
- mitochondrial metabolism

…and never hints that SCFAs will later take up half the review.

This is why readers feel like they’re reading:

1. A metabolic Treg review  
2. Then suddenly a SCFA review written by someone else  

Section 2 is unintentionally “selfish” — it does not leave conceptual space for Section 4.

---

# ⭐ **ULTIMATE VERDICT ON SECTION 2**  
**From the perspective of the abstract + introduction:**  
✔ Section 2 is consistent  
✔ Mechanistically strong  
✔ Sets up Section 3  
✖ Does NOT prepare for SCFAs  
✖ Over-emphasizes PPARγ  
✖ Makes the review feel split into two unrelated halves  
✖ Fails to create a unifying metabolic framework

Section 2 is technically excellent,  
but architecturally it is one of the sources of the paper’s **split identity**.

This is why the review feels like **two papers in one**:

- **Paper A:** PPARγ → FAO → Tregs  
- **Paper B:** SCFAs → HDAC → innate cells  

Section 2 creates Paper A,  
but doesn’t anticipate Paper B.

# ⭐ **SECTION 3 (All subsections) — What it Contains**

Section 3 is broken into:

### **3.1 FAO fuels Treg cells**  
- Tregs rely on FAO  
- CD36 uptake → FAO → Foxp3  
- AMPK → CPT1A → FAO  
- FAO enhances IL-10, TGF-β secretion  
- Chinese herbal formula → AMPK activation → FAO → Treg↑ / Th17↓  

### **3.2 PPARγ promotes Treg differentiation via FAO**  
- PPARγ agonists induce Tregs  
- PPARγ → CD36/CPT1A → FAO → Foxp3  
- PPARγ enhances IL-10, TGF-β  

### **3.3 FAO suppresses glycolysis + Th17 differentiation**  
- FAO pushes cells toward Tregs  
- Glycolysis pushes cells toward Th17  
- Lactate → RORγT  
- FAO-driven metabolic shift suppresses Th17  
- AMPK vs mTOR  
- Links to psoriasis models  

### **3.4 PPARγ inhibits Th17 differentiation via FAO**  
- PPARγ agonists reduce IL-17, IL-22  
- PPARγ suppresses RORγT  
- PPARγ deficiency → IL-17 ↑  
- Concludes PPARγ restrains Th17 cytokines

---

# ⭐ **1. Does Section 3 logically follow from Section 2 + Intro?**  
**Yes — and extremely well.**

The introduction said:

- Tregs rely on FAO  
- Th17 rely on glycolysis  
- PPARγ regulates FAO  
- Psoriasis = Treg/Th17 imbalance  

Section 2 expanded PPARγ–FAO machinery.

Section 3 now applies that machinery to:

- Tregs (3.1, 3.2)  
- Th17 (3.3, 3.4)

This is exactly the natural flow.

### **Verdict:**  
**Narratively coherent from Section 1 → Section 2 → Section 3.  
This is the most unified part of the paper.**

---

# ⭐ **2. Does Section 3 match the abstract?**  
**Yes — perfectly.**

The abstract promised:

- FAO supports Treg development  
- Glycolysis supports Th17  
- PPARγ shifts balance via FAO  
- SCFAs later modulate this axis  
- Psoriasis inflammation linked to this imbalance  

Section 3 delivers:

- FAO → Treg ↑  
- Glycolysis → Th17 ↑  
- PPARγ is the metabolic switch  
- AMPK, CPT1, CD36  
- IL-10/TGF-β vs IL-17/IL-22  
- Psoretic inflammation tied to this metabolic axis

Section 3 is exactly what the abstract frames.

---

# ⭐ **3. Is Section 3 internally coherent?**  
**Yes — except for one conceptual over-claim.**

The entire section argues:

> **FAO → Treg differentiation → anti-inflammatory  
Glycolysis → Th17 differentiation → pro-inflammatory  
PPARγ → FAO → psoriasis relief**

This is metabolically correct,  
but it implicitly creates an **over-simplified binary model**:

- FAO = good  
- Glycolysis = bad  

In reality:

- effector Tregs also need glycolysis  
- Th17 cells also use fatty acid synthesis and glutaminolysis  
- PPARγ is not the master regulator of FAO in T cells  
- PPARδ and AMPK are more central  

But for a review aimed at mechanistic clarity, this simplification is not fatal — it just needs careful framing.

### **Verdict:**  
**Strong mechanistic clarity. Slight biological over-generalization.**

---

# ⭐ **4. Structural coherence: where Section 3 begins to drift**

Even though Section 3 is internally consistent, it does create **two problems for the global manuscript architecture**:

---

## ❌ **Problem 1: Section 3 keeps everything in the ADAPTIVE immunity world**

Section 3 intensifies the Treg/Th17 focus, which matches the intro and abstract but **moves the manuscript farther away** from:

- innate immunity  
- neutrophils  
- macrophages  
- dendritic cells  
- microbiome interactions  
- environmental fatty acids  
- SCFA receptor signaling  

Thus, Section 3 deepens one arm of the review (PPARγ–Treg),  
but leaves no conceptual openings for SCFAs.

SCFAs, in reality, affect:

- HDAC inhibition  
- GPCRs on innate cells  
- tolerogenic DCs  
- macrophage polarization  
- neutrophil chemotaxis  

None of which are set up in Section 3.

---

## ❌ **Problem 2: Section 3 does NOT prepare for Section 4 (SCFAs)**

After Section 3, the reader expects:

- a continuation of FAO  
- more about PPARγ  
- links to psoriasis models  
- FAO in keratinocytes  
- FA metabolism in immune cells  
- maybe lipidomic evidence  

But Section 4 suddenly shifts to:

**SCFAs as gut-derived microbial metabolites.**  
- different vocabulary  
- different receptors  
- different mechanisms  
- different literature  

There is NO conceptual bridge.

The reader gets narrative whiplash.

---

# ⭐ **5. Scientific issue: mis-centering FAO as the “master switch”**
Sections 3.1–3.4 repeatedly reinforce the message:

> “FAO is the master regulator of Treg/Th17 balance.”

But this is not fully accurate:

- FAO is supportive, not determinative.  
- TCR signal strength, cytokine milieu, and epigenetics dominate differentiation.  
- SCFAs act mainly via HDAC and GPCRs, not FAO.  

Thus, the FAO centricity makes later SCFA mechanisms feel less integrated.

---

# ⭐ **6. Narrative issue: Section 3 is TOO good at one thing**

It creates a beautifully coherent metabolic story…  
…for HALF the review.

But it does not “share narrative space” with:

- SCFAs  
- innate immunity  
- microbiome–skin axis  
- therapeutics

So when the manuscript later tries to pivot to these topics,  
the pivot is not smooth.

---

# ⭐ **ULTIMATE VERDICT ON SECTION 3**

**Strengths:**
- Excellent mechanistic detail  
- Perfect continuation of Section 2  
- Perfect alignment with the abstract and introduction  
- Solid immunometabolic reasoning  
- Clear, organized, readable  
- Strong evidence for PPARγ–FAO–Treg axis  

**Weaknesses (structural):**
- Deepens a narrow metabolic focus without preparing the SCFA half  
- Entirely adaptive immunity; no innate–metabolic crosstalk  
- Makes SCFA sections feel like a separate paper  
- Overemphasizes FAO as central mechanism  
- No hint of microbiome or fatty acid–environment interactions  

**In summary:**  
**Section 3 is excellent, but it strengthens the “split personality” of the review.**  
It makes the reader feel like the paper is *about Treg metabolism* and nothing else — which makes the SCFA and innate sections feel bolted-on.

---

# **Reviewer Report**

The manuscript presents an ambitious attempt to synthesize metabolic and microbial mechanisms—specifically PPARγ-driven fatty acid oxidation (FAO) and short-chain fatty acid (SCFA) immunomodulation—in the context of psoriasis. While the topic is timely and mechanistically appealing, the work suffers from substantial structural and conceptual misalignment that must be addressed before it can be meaningfully considered for publication.

## **1. Major Conceptual Misalignment Between Title and Content**

The title, *“Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk in Psoriasis”*, promises a review focused on **innate immune biology** and **lipid-mediated signaling**—a compelling direction, given the expanding literature on keratinocyte metabolic stress, neutrophil-derived NETs, macrophage polarization, and dendritic cell–driven IL-23 production.

However, the manuscript itself is **overwhelmingly centered on adaptive immunity**, particularly:

- Treg/Th17 balance,  
- Foxp3 regulation,  
- FAO-driven Treg differentiation,  
- PPARγ signaling in CD4⁺ T cells.

This creates a profound conceptual disconnect. The innate immune components appear only much later in the manuscript, introduced abruptly and without prior scaffolding, making the paper feel like a fusion of two unrelated reviews. Readers expecting a discussion of lipid-innate pathways will be misled.

A re-titling of the article or a major restructuring of the manuscript is required.

---

## **2. Strengths and Positive Qualities (Under a Different Title)**

If this manuscript were titled along the lines of:

### **“PPARγ-Driven Fatty Acid Oxidation and SCFA-Mediated Regulation of the Treg/Th17 Axis in Psoriasis”**

then **Sections 2 and 3** would represent the strongest components of the work.

### **Strengths of Sections 2–3:**

- **Mechanistic accuracy** in describing FAO machinery (CD36, FATPs, CPT1A, AMPK, PGC-1α).  
- **Clear immunometabolic explanations** of how FAO supports Treg lineage stability and suppresses glycolysis-driven Th17 development.  
- **Logical integration** of PPARγ with Treg metabolic programming.  
- **Well-selected literature** supporting FAO-dependent transcriptional and epigenetic regulation (Foxp3 acetylation, anti-inflammatory cytokine output).  
- **Readable and well-structured narrative** that moves coherently from metabolic pathways to immune consequences.

These sections are, in isolation, a high-quality scientific review of adaptive immunometabolism in psoriasis.  
They simply do not belong to the title or the later parts of the manuscript.

---

## **3. Abrupt and Poorly Integrated Transition to Clinical Interventions (Section 5)**

The manuscript abruptly shifts into a broad catalog of **clinical and preclinical interventions**, including:

- topical SCFA formulations,  
- SCFA derivatives,  
- probiotic extracts,  
- dietary fibers producing SCFAs,  
- even cheese consumption.

This section appears **entirely disconnected from the mechanistic work** laid out in Sections 2–4.

### Problems:

- **No mechanistic bridge** from FAO/PPARγ pathways to these therapeutic proposals.  
- **Heterogeneous and unstructured** collection of preclinical data (IMQ models, murine fiber studies, in vitro SCFA derivatives).  
- **Therapies unrelated to the metabolic axis** presented earlier.  
- No explanation of why **acetate exacerbates** IMQ lesions while **butyrate ameliorates** them.  
- **No connection** between microbiome-derived SCFA interventions and the previously emphasized FAO machinery.

As written, Section 5 feels like it was imported from an unrelated SCFA dermatology review. It should either be rewritten with mechanistic grounding or removed entirely.

---

## **4. Overreliance on Non-Psoriasis and Non-Human Literature**

A large proportion of the cited studies:

- come from **oncology**,  
- derive from **HIV immunometabolism**,  
- study **colitis**,  
- or use **generic T-cell activation systems**.

The manuscript rarely makes clear which findings:

- are derived from **psoriatic tissue**,  
- stem from **human subjects**,  
- or belong to **IMQ mouse models**,  
- versus those from unrelated disease systems.

This lack of transparency weakens the translational relevance.  
A dedicated paragraph acknowledging limitations of cross-disease extrapolation is necessary.

---

## **5. Missing Key Scientific Players (Minor but Significant Technical Issues)**

A few mechanistic gaps and oversimplifications should be corrected:

### **A. Omission of PPARδ**
PPARδ is the **dominant regulator of FAO in T cells**, far more so than PPARγ.  
This is not mentioned at all, and creates an inaccurate impression of PPARγ being the master metabolic switch.

### **B. Oversimplified FAO vs glycolysis dichotomy**
The manuscript presents:

- FAO → Treg (good)  
- Glycolysis → Th17 (bad)

This binary is biologically simplistic:

- Tregs require glycolytic bursts for proliferation.  
- Th17 cells use glutaminolysis and fatty acid synthesis.  
- Metabolic programming is context-dependent.

Oversimplification is acceptable for readability, but requires explicit disclaimers for accuracy.

### **C. SCFAs do not physiologically activate PPARγ**
This connection is implied but unsupported.

### **D. Failed distinction between innate and adaptive effects**
Innate SCFA mechanisms (HDAC inhibition, GPCRs, DC tolerogenicity) are distinct from adaptive FAO pathways and should be clearly separated conceptually.

These corrections are manageable if the paper is revised.

---

## **6. Overall Recommendation**

### **Recommendation: Major Revision**

The manuscript contains high-quality mechanistic content (Sections 2–3) but suffers from:

- conceptual misalignment between title, abstract, and content  
- abrupt and unintegrated therapeutic proposals  
- overuse of non-psoriasis and non-human literature  
- underdeveloped innate immunity component  
- missing mechanistic connections (PPARδ, epigenetic vs metabolic regulation)  

With substantial restructuring—and potentially a new title—the manuscript could become a solid contribution to the field of psoriasis immunometabolism.

A thoughtful reorganization, a clearer narrative spine, and a more focused scope will be essential for reconsideration.

---

- rewrite the review in a more formal Frontiers template, or  
- generate the “Major Revision Roadmap,” showing exactly how to restructure the manuscript for resubmission.

# **Reviewer Report (Condensed)**

The manuscript addresses metabolic and microbial regulation of psoriasis through PPARγ-driven FAO and SCFA-mediated immunomodulation. While the topic is timely, there are major structural inconsistencies that must be resolved before further consideration.

## **1. Title–Content Mismatch**
The title promises a review of **fatty acid–innate immune crosstalk**, yet the manuscript focuses predominantly on **adaptive immunity**, especially Treg/Th17 balance and T-cell metabolism. Innate immunity appears only later and without preparatory context. The work, as written, does not deliver the topic advertised.

## **2. Strengths (Under a Different Title)**
If retitled to reflect **T-cell immunometabolism**—for example:  
**“PPARγ-Driven Fatty Acid Oxidation and SCFA-Mediated Modulation of the Treg/Th17 Axis in Psoriasis”**—then **Sections 2 and 3** are well-written, well-referenced, and mechanistically coherent. They accurately describe FAO, PPARγ signaling, and metabolic control of Treg/Th17 differentiation.

## **3. Abrupt, Weakly Connected Therapeutic Section**
Section 5 introduces topical SCFAs, fiber supplementation, probiotic extracts, and even cheese intake. These clinical/preclinical interventions are **poorly integrated**, lack mechanistic connection to the preceding sections, and feel like elements from a different review. A clearer rationale—or removal—is required.

## **4. Overreliance on Non-Psoriasis and Non-Human Literature**
Much of the cited work comes from cancer biology, colitis, HIV studies, and generic T-cell systems. The manuscript does not distinguish clearly between human, murine, and non-psoriatic findings. This limits the clinical relevance and should be explicitly acknowledged.

## **5. Minor Scientific Issues**
- **PPARδ**, the primary FAO regulator in T cells, is not discussed.  
- The FAO vs glycolysis dichotomy is scientifically **oversimplified**.  
- The manuscript implies SCFAs act through PPARγ, which is not strongly supported.  

These issues are correctable upon revision.

---

## **Recommendation: Major Revision**

The manuscript contains valuable mechanistic content but requires significant restructuring to align scope, title, and narrative. A revised, more focused version may merit reconsideration.

# **Reviewer Report – Recommendation: Major Revision**

The manuscript presents an interesting attempt to integrate metabolic immunology—specifically PPARγ-mediated fatty acid oxidation (FAO) and short-chain fatty acid (SCFA) signaling—in the context of psoriasis. While the topic is meaningful and several sections are well written, the manuscript in its current form suffers from structural, conceptual, and scope-related inconsistencies that preclude acceptance without substantial revision.

## **1. Title–Content Misalignment**
The title promises a focus on *fatty acid–innate immune crosstalk*, yet the manuscript is dominated by **adaptive immunometabolism**, particularly Treg/Th17 dynamics and T-cell FAO. Innate immune pathways (DCs, macrophages, neutrophils) appear abruptly in later sections without proper framing and do not reflect the central thrust of the manuscript. The work either requires a new title or a substantial restructuring to align content with the stated focus.

## **2. Strong but Misplaced Mechanistic Sections**
Sections 2 and 3 provide a clear, well-referenced account of PPARγ-driven FAO, Treg metabolic programming, and the glycolysis–Th17 axis. Under a different title—e.g., *“Metabolic Regulation of the Treg/Th17 Axis in Psoriasis: Roles of PPARγ and SCFAs”*—these sections would be highly appropriate. In the current configuration, however, they contribute to the disconnect between the manuscript’s stated scope and its actual emphasis.

## **3. Abrupt and Weakly Integrated Therapeutic Section**
Section 5 introduces topical SCFAs, SCFA derivatives, probiotics, dietary fibers, and even cheese consumption. These interventions are **loosely connected** to the mechanistic material and presented without a unifying framework. The section feels appended rather than integrated, and requires either removal or a mechanistic rewrite linked to the FAO/SCFA pathways established earlier.

## **4. Limited Psoriasis-Specific and Human-Derived Evidence**
A considerable portion of the cited literature is **not psoriasis-focused**, and often derives from cancer biology, colitis, HIV studies, or generic T-cell systems. The manuscript does not distinguish clearly between human, murine, and non-dermatologic findings. The limitations of extrapolating these data to psoriasis should be discussed explicitly.

## **5. Minor Scientific Issues**
- Important regulators such as **PPARδ**, a key FAO controller in T cells, are absent.  
- The FAO–glycolysis dichotomy is presented **too simplistically**, ignoring context-dependent metabolic flexibility.  
- The manuscript occasionally implies SCFAs act via PPARγ, which lacks strong evidence.  

These are correctable but must be addressed.

---

# **Recommendation**
**Major Revision**

The manuscript contains strong mechanistic components but requires substantial restructuring to resolve title–content misalignment, integrate SCFA and innate immune sections coherently, and ensure therapeutic proposals are mechanistically grounded. Addressing these issues will significantly improve clarity, scientific accuracy, and overall impact.

### 98. **ChatGPT:**

Nice. Let’s autopsy this title properly.

> **Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk in Psoriasis**

We’ll:
1. Break it into components  
2. Give etymology where useful  
3. Define the *closed terminology* (what each word commits you to, scientifically)

---

## 1. **“Fatty Acid”**

### Etymology  
- **Fatty**: from *fat* (Proto-Germanic *fettą*), originally “fattened, plump; rich in fat.”  
- **Acid**: from Latin *acidus* = “sour, sharp”.

Originally: *“sour/oily substance”* → chemically: a molecule that donates protons (H⁺).

### Biological / biochemical meaning  
**Fatty acid** = a molecule with:
- a **hydrocarbon chain** (–(CH₂)ₙ–)  
- a **terminal carboxyl group** (–COOH)

In biology:
- Stored in triglycerides, phospholipids, cholesteryl esters  
- Oxidized in mitochondria and peroxisomes for ATP (β-oxidation)  
- Act as **ligands** for nuclear receptors (PPARs), **substrates** for eicosanoids, ceramides, etc.

### Implied *closed terminology* in a title  
Using **“Fatty Acid”** in a title implicitly commits you to discuss:
- **Types**: saturated, monounsaturated, polyunsaturated  
- **Metabolism**: uptake (CD36, FATP), FAO (CPT1, CPT2), lipogenesis  
- **Signaling**: PPARs, TLRs, NLRP3, lipid mediators (eicosanoids, resolvins, etc.)  
- **Cellular context**: keratinocytes, macrophages, DCs, etc.

You’re not just talking “fats”; you’re signaling **specific metabolic and signaling pathways**.

---

## 2. **“Innate Immune”**

### Etymology  
- **Innate**: from Latin *innatus* = “inborn, native, natural”.  
- **Immune**: from Latin *immunis* = “exempt, free from burden” (originally legal/tax immunity → then disease immunity).

### Biological meaning  
**Innate immunity** = the ancient, fast, non-specific arm of the immune system.

Includes:
- **Cells**: neutrophils, macrophages, dendritic cells, NK cells, ILCs, mast cells, eosinophils, basophils  
- **Molecules**: complement, acute-phase proteins, antimicrobial peptides  
- **Receptors**: PRRs (TLRs, NLRs, RLRs, CLRs)

Key properties:
- Germline-encoded  
- Rapid response  
- No (or limited) antigen-specific memory  
- **Set the stage** for adaptive immunity (IL-12, IL-23, IL-1, Type I IFNs).

### Closed terminology implications  
By saying **“innate immune”** in a title, you are promising to focus on:

- **Innate cell types**, not mainly Treg/Th17  
- Pattern recognition (TLR7/9, TLR2/4, NLRP3)  
- Early cytokine bursts (TNF, IL-1, IL-6, IL-23, IFN-α)  
- Things like NETs, inflammasomes, DC activation, macrophage polarization.

If the paper then spends 70% of its time on CD4⁺ T cells, reviewers feel bait-and-switched.

---

## 3. **“Crosstalk”**

### Etymology  
- **Cross**: here in sense of “mutual, intersecting, reciprocal”.  
- **Talk**: obviously “communication”.  
The compound “crosstalk” originated in telecom: unwanted mixing of signals between lines; then adopted in biology as “communication between pathways”.

### Biological meaning  
**Crosstalk** = bidirectional or multi-directional communication between systems.  

In immunometabolism:
- Metabolic pathways ↔ signaling pathways  
- Innate cells ↔ adaptive cells  
- Tissue cells (keratinocytes, adipocytes) ↔ immune cells  
- Host metabolism ↔ microbiome metabolites

### Closed implications  
If your title says “crosstalk”, you’re promising:

- **Two-way** interactions, not a simple “fatty acids → innate cells” one-liner.  
- Some **feedback circuits**: e.g., inflammation altering lipid metabolism, and lipids reshaping inflammation.  
- Some kind of **integrated model**, not just two disjoint literatures in parallel.

If the structure is more like:
> part A: fatty acids  
> part B: innate immunity  
> part C: random SCFA therapies  
that’s not crosstalk – that’s coexistence.

---

## 4. **“in Psoriasis”**

### Etymology  
- From Greek **ψωρίασις** (*psōriasis*), related to *ψώρα* (*psōra*) = “itch, mange, scab”.

### Biological/clinical meaning  
**Psoriasis** = chronic, immune-mediated inflammatory skin disease with:
- Hyperproliferative, poorly differentiating keratinocytes  
- IL-23/IL-17 axis activation  
- Strong involvement of DCs, neutrophils, and T cells  
- Systemic comorbidities (metabolic syndrome, cardiovascular risk, NAFLD)

### Closed implications  
Saying **“in psoriasis”** means:
- Pathways discussed should be demonstrably involved in **psoriatic disease**,  
  not just “somewhere in immunology.”  
- Ideally: data from **psoriatic tissue, psoriatic patients, or psoriasis models**,  
  and clear explanation when extrapolating from other diseases.

If most references are from:
- cancer,  
- colitis,  
- HIV,  
- generic T-cell systems,  
and psoriasis is used mainly as a conceptual backdrop, reviewers will feel that “in psoriasis” is overclaiming.

---

## 5. What the Full Title *Actually Commits You To*

> **“Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk in Psoriasis”**

In one line, this promises:

1. **Primary players**:  
   - Fatty acids and their metabolism  
   - Innate immune cells (DCs, macrophages, neutrophils, perhaps keratinocytes)  

2. **Primary relationships**:  
   - How fatty acids *shape* innate immune activation and function in psoriasis  
   - How innate inflammation *feeds back* into fatty acid handling (uptake, oxidation, lipogenesis, lipid mediators)

3. **Context**:  
   - This interplay is occurring specifically in **psoriatic skin** (and perhaps systemic milieu), not generic inflammation.

4. **Type of article**:  
   - Conceptual, integrative review with an emphasis on **mechanistic diagrams**, **pathway integration**, and **psoriasis-specific evidence**.

Your current manuscript, in contrast, mostly does:

- FAO/PPARγ → **Treg/Th17** (adaptive)  
- SCFAs → Tregs + innate cells (but mostly via epigenetics)  
- Therapeutic SCFA/probiotic/fiber snippets only loosely tied back.

So, structurally, your paper’s content aligns more with:

> “Immunometabolic Regulation of the Treg/Th17 Axis in Psoriasis: Roles of PPARγ-Mediated Fatty Acid Oxidation and Microbial Short-Chain Fatty Acids”

than with “Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk”.

---

If you like, next step is:

- Use this broken-down terminology to **rebuild a precise, honest new title**,  
or  
- Take each word (“fatty acid”, “innate”, “crosstalk”) and design **bullet commitments** your revised manuscript must meet if you want to keep them.

# **The Abstract (Deconstructed)**

> *Psoriasis is a chronic inflammatory skin disorder driven by dysregulation of the Treg/Th17 axis…*

> *Emerging evidence highlights peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor γ (PPARγ) as a key regulator of fatty acid oxidation (FAO)…*

> *Concurrently, short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs)… ameliorate psoriatic inflammation…*

> *This review synthesizes mechanistic insights into PPARγ-driven FAO and SCFA-mediated immunomodulation…*

We’ll handle each conceptual block separately.

---

# ⭐ **1. “Psoriasis is a chronic inflammatory skin disorder driven by dysregulation of the Treg/Th17 axis.”**

## **Etymology**
- **Chronic**: Greek *chronos* = time; long-lasting.  
- **Inflammatory**: Latin *inflammare* = to set on fire.  
- **Dysregulation**: *dys-* (bad, impaired) + regulation.  

## **Closed terminology obligations**
Saying psoriasis is driven by **Treg/Th17 dysregulation** means your review must:

- Focus on **adaptive immunity**, not innate.  
- Explain **IL-17, IL-22, IL-23** pathways.  
- Discuss **Foxp3**, **RORγT**, **TGF-β**, **IL-10**, **IL-6**, **IL-23**.  
- Present **metabolic distinctions** between these lineages (FAO vs glycolysis).

This first sentence already commits you to writing an **adaptive immunometabolism review**, not an innate lipid signaling review.

This is why your title conflicts with your content.

---

# ⭐ **2. “PPARγ as a key regulator of fatty acid oxidation (FAO)”**

## **Etymology**
- **Peroxisome proliferator–activated receptor (PPAR)**  
  - *Peroxisome*: Greek *peri* (around) + *oxys* (sharp/acid) → organelle doing oxidative metabolism.  
  - *Proliferator*: Latin *proles* (offspring) + *ferre* (to bear) → cell growth.  
  - *Activated receptor*: ligand-induced transcription factor.  

- **Oxidation**: Latin *oxygēnum* (acid-former) + suffix *-ation*.  

## **Closed terminology obligations**
Calling PPARγ a **key** regulator of FAO commits you to:

- FA uptake pathways (CD36, FATPs)  
- Mitochondrial import (CPT1, CPT2)  
- β-oxidation biochemistry  
- PGC-1α and AMPK interactions  
- Showing FAO is **functional in psoriasis**, not just generic immunology  
- Discussing **PPARδ**, unless you explicitly justify its omission  

By centering PPARγ so strongly, the abstract tells reviewers:

> “This is a mechanistic metabolic immunology review.”

This raises the standard for accuracy.

---

# ⭐ **3. “PPARγ activation enhances FAO while suppressing glycolysis”**

## **Etymology**
- **Glycolysis**: Greek *glykys* (sweet) + *lysis* (to break).  

## **Closed terminology obligations**
When you invoke FAO ↔ glycolysis antagonism, you must:

- Discuss **AMPK** and **mTOR** as metabolic switches.  
- Address cross-pathway regulation (malonyl-CoA, ACC phosphorylation).  
- Explain that Th17 cells rely on **glycolysis**, not FAO.  
- Explain **how PPARγ suppresses glycolysis** (biochemically difficult!).  

This is scientifically tricky:  
PPARγ does *not* strongly suppress glycolysis in T cells.

So this statement commits you to a mechanism you cannot fully support.

This creates later conflict.

---

# ⭐ **4. “SCFAs… ameliorate psoriatic inflammation by promoting Treg expansion, inhibiting Th17 polarization, and modulating innate immune cells…”**

## **Etymology**
- **Short-chain fatty acids**: “fatty acid” (see earlier) + “short chain” = ≤6 carbons.  
- **Polarization**: Latin *polus* (axis) → directional differentiation.  

## **Closed terminology obligations**
Including SCFAs in the abstract obligates you to:

- Discuss **SCFA transport** (MCT1, SMCT1).  
- Describe **SCFA receptors** (GPR41/43/109A).  
- Cover **HDAC inhibition**.  
- Explain SCFA effects on **Tregs + Th17 + innate cells**.  
- Link SCFAs to **psoriasis-specific evidence**.

This is now **a second metabolic system**, unrelated to FAO/PPARγ.

But your abstract implies you will **unify** them.

This forces the manuscript to provide:

> A single mechanistic model integrating PPARγ–FAO and SCFAs.

Which the manuscript does not do.

Thus, the abstract overcommits the paper.

---

# ⭐ **5. “SCFAs exert their effects through receptor-dependent signaling and epigenetic mechanisms (HDAC inhibition)”**

## **Closed terminology obligations**
This line obligates you to:

- Elaborate receptor biology  
- Explain GPCR-dependent cascades  
- Discuss HDAC inhibition → histone acetylation → Foxp3 expression  
- Integrate epigenetics with metabolism  
- Address innate vs adaptive downstream effects  

Again: you are committing to **two separate mechanism classes**:

- epigenetics  
- receptor signaling  

This diverges sharply from FAO/cellular metabolism.

Thus, you commit yourself to writing a **hybrid metabolic–epigenetic review**, which is harder to unify.

---

# ⭐ **6. “This review synthesizes mechanistic insights into PPARγ-driven FAO and SCFA-mediated immunomodulation…”**

This sentence is the **thesis statement**, and it commits you to:

### **A dual-immunometabolic model** of psoriasis integrating:  
1. **Endogenous lipid metabolism (PPARγ → FAO)**  
2. **Microbial metabolite signaling (SCFAs → GPCR → HDAC)**  

To fulfill this promise, the manuscript must show:

- How FAO + SCFAs intersect  
- How they jointly modulate Treg/Th17  
- How they shape inflammation specifically in psoriasis  
- Why these two pathways are being reviewed together  

Your manuscript **never provides a unifying model**, which is why Section 4–5 feel detached.

---

# ⭐ **7. “Proposing metabolic and microbiome-targeted strategies for psoriasis treatment.”**

This final line obligates you to:

- Translate FAO/PPARγ into actual therapeutic proposals  
- Translate SCFA biology into therapeutic strategies  
- Ensure all therapies emerge **directly from the mechanistic sections**  

Instead, the manuscript offers:

- probiotics  
- cheese consumption  
- inulin  
- SCFA derivatives  
- topical butyrate  
- etc.

These do **not** feel mechanistically grounded.

This is where reviewers sense disjointedness.

---

Here is the distilled conclusion of the autopsy:

### The abstract promises:
- a dual-pathway metabolic immunology review (PPARγ–FAO + SCFAs)  
- an integrated mechanistic synthesis  
- psoriasis-specific immunometabolism  
- therapeutic proposals arising logically from the mechanisms  

### But the manuscript delivers:

**TWO REVIEWS + A THERAPEUTIC SHOPPING LIST**  
instead of **ONE INTEGRATED REVIEW**.

# ⭐ **1. What the Introduction Actually Does (Content Breakdown)**

Your introduction contains four major moves:

### **(1) Defines psoriasis as a chronic inflammatory skin disease**

— Fine.

### **(2) Establishes the Treg/Th17 imbalance as the central pathogenic axis**

This immediately signals to readers that the **entire rest of the review will focus on adaptive immunity**, not innate immunity.

### **(3) Introduces immunometabolism (FAO for Tregs, glycolysis for Th17)**  
This is good and mechanistically accurate.  
It shows the authors understand the metabolic “lineage hardwiring” concept.

This places FAO and glycolysis at center-stage and primes the reader for:

- PPARγ  
- FAO  
- metabolic programming  
- Foxp3  
- RORγT  
- T-cell lineage work

### **(4) Introduces PPARγ as a regulator of FAO and potential therapeutic target**  
This is consistent with the narrative, but again:

**PPARγ → FAO → Treg/Th17 is an adaptive immunometabolic loop.**

### **(5) Finishes with a sentence promising discussion of SCFAs**  
This is where the first visible fracture appears:

> *“This review elucidates the regulatory role of PPARγ in fatty acid metabolism, and summarizes the relationship between short-chain fatty acids and psoriasis…”*

The introduction switches abruptly from nuclear FAO metabolism (**PPARγ**) to **gut-derived SCFAs**, which are not metabolically similar, not in the same pathway, and have no shared cell-type context yet.

This is the first sign that the review is *stitched*, not integrated.

---

# ⭐ **2. What the Introduction *Commits* You To (Closed Terminology Obligations)**

Your introduction basically locks the reader into THREE promises:

---

### ✔ **Promise 1: This is a review of ADAPTIVE IMMUNITY**
Because you spend the whole introduction on:

- Tregs  
- Th17  
- Foxp3  
- RORγT

- metabolic pathways of T cells  

This commits the paper to focusing on:

**T-cell immunometabolism → psoriasis**

This is strong and clear.  
This is also NOT what the title claims (“innate immune crosstalk”).

Reviewer flags that immediately.

---

### ✔ **Promise 2: This review will unify immunometabolism around PPARγ-driven FAO**

You introduce PPARγ as the *central metabolic rheostat*.

This obligates you to:

- provide strong psoriasis-specific PPARγ evidence  
- show FA uptake, FAO, CD36, CPT1 involvement in psoriatic immunity  
- explain the adaptive immune consequences  
- describe metabolic circuitries (AMPK, mTOR)  

This part is strong and the paper actually delivers it (Section 2–3).

The introduction implies a **shared mechanistic arc**:

PPARγ → FAO → Tregs  
SCFAs → immunomodulation → Tregs

- Explain how these two systems converge  
- Show SCFAs interacting with lipid metabolism (they don’t much)  
- Show SCFAs influencing psoriasis through FAO or PPARγ (weak evidence)  
- Build a bridge section uniting the two halves  

The manuscript **never constructs this bridge**, so the introduction feels like a false promise.

Introduced as a metabolic regulator → mechanistic reasoning is consistent.

Psoriasis → Treg/Th17 → immunometabolism → FAO → PPARγ is a coherent chain.

*Under the correct title*, this introduction would be **excellent**.

Not a single innate immune cell type is introduced.

This is a major conceptual mismatch.

A reviewer sees this and immediately doubts the manuscript’s integrity.

SCFAs belong to:

- microbiome  
- gut–skin axis

- epigenetic regulation

PPARγ belongs to:

- endogenous fatty acid ligands

These are **two entirely different universes**.

Yet the introduction places them side-by-side **as if they are part of the same pathway**.

This is structurally misleading.

---

But the manuscript never integrates:

- FAO metabolism

---

### ⭐ **❌ 4. No rationale is given for combining PPARγ–FAO and SCFAs.**

A reviewer will ask:

> What’s the unifying hypothesis?”

The introduction does not answer.

This reveals the core structural issue in the entire manuscript.

### 123. **ChatGPT:**

# **Reviewer Comment (Refined Version)**

The manuscript by *et al.*, titled **“Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk in Psoriasis,”** exhibits moments of scientific rigor, yet fails to deliver a coherent or unified narrative. The central problem begins with the title itself. Although the authors promise to explore crosstalk between fatty acid metabolism and **innate immunity**, the manuscript instead devotes the majority of its attention to **adaptive immunometabolism**, particularly the role of **PPARγ-driven fatty acid oxidation (FAO)** in shaping the **Treg/Th17 axis**.  

Even within this adaptive framework, the literature base is poorly curated. Many of the mechanistic references derive from oncology, metabolic disease, or other unrelated fields, yet the manuscript does not acknowledge these extrapolations. The result is an impression that psoriasis is only tangentially supported by the cited work, with no clear boundaries between established dermatologic evidence and mechanistic speculation from external models.

The later introduction of **innate immune components** (neutrophils, dendritic cells, macrophages) arrives abruptly and without conceptual integration. These sections read as if they were written for an entirely different review and subsequently attached, rather than emerging naturally from the PPARγ/FAO narrative developed earlier. No metabolic or mechanistic bridge is constructed, and the innate immune material does not converse with the FAO/T-cell framework in any meaningful way.

This fragmentation becomes more pronounced in the **clinical application** section. Here, one would expect a clear mechanistic translation—either direct therapeutic implications of PPARγ-FAO pathways or a well-reasoned explanation of how SCFA-based or microbiome-targeted interventions relate to the immunometabolic mechanisms described. Instead, the section feels disconnected, eclectic, and insufficiently justified. The therapeutic proposals appear “out of the blue,” lacking anchoring in either psoriasis-specific data or the mechanisms emphasized earlier in the manuscript.

In sum, the manuscript suffers from a structural and conceptual disjunction: a title that misrepresents the focus, a “Frankenstein” assembly of independent parts (Sections 2–3 vs. Section 4), and a clinical section that does not arise organically from the mechanistic content. As currently written, the manuscript is **not suitable for publication**. It would require a complete reconceptualization—either retitling and restructuring around adaptive immunometabolism, or rebuilding the entire narrative to genuinely address fatty-acid–innate immune crosstalk—before it could be considered for serious review.

# **Reviewer Comment (Human-Style, Imperfect)**

The article by *et al.*, titled **“Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk in Psoriasis,”** gives at first the impression that the authors tried to work with serious scientific material. But after reading it more carefully, the whole piece feels disconnected and actually quite confusing.

To begin with, the title does not match the content. The paper hardly deals with innate immunity at all. Instead, most of it focuses on **adaptive immunity**, especially the effects of PPARγ and fatty-acid oxidation on the **Treg/Th17 balance**. This is not a problem by itself, but it becomes one because the reader expects something very different based on the title.

Another issue is the references. A big part of them come from cancer studies or other unrelated conditions. This could be fine if the authors explained why they are using them, but they don’t. So the reader is left wondering how much of the paper actually applies to psoriasis and how much is borrowed from other fields.

Later in the manuscript, there is suddenly a big shift into **innate immunity** (neutrophils, dendritic cells, macrophages). This section appears without any proper connection to the previous adaptive-immunity parts. It feels like a separate mini-review that was pasted into the manuscript, not something naturally following the earlier arguments.

Finally, the section on **clinical applications** adds more confusion. Instead of building on the mechanisms described before, it introduces ideas and treatments that don’t clearly relate to PPARγ, FAO, or even psoriasis in a consistent way. There is little explanation of how these therapies link back to the biology presented earlier.

Overall, the manuscript reads like **different pieces written at different times**, put together without a clear plan. The mismatch between the title and the content, the unrelated references, the abrupt jump between topics, and the weak clinical section make it hard to follow. In its current form, the paper is not ready for publication and would need major rewriting to become a coherent and meaningful review.

# **Reviewer Comment (Human, Professor-like, Slightly Imperfect)**

The manuscript by *et al.*, titled **“Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk in Psoriasis,”** aims high but does not land where it intends to. There is some solid scientific effort here, but the overall presentation feels uneven, and in several places honestly confusing.

To start with, the title promises a discussion about fatty acids interacting with the *innate* immune system in psoriasis. But most of the manuscript focuses on **adaptive immunity**, especially the PPARγ–FAO pathway and its effects on the Treg/Th17 balance. These parts are actually written with care, but they don’t match what the title is claiming. This mismatch makes the reader expect a different paper than the one delivered.

A second problem is with the references. Many of them come from oncology, generic T-cell immunology, or metabolism in completely unrelated diseases. Using outside literature is fine if the authors clarify why they are borrowing it, but here there is almost no such explanation. The leap from cancer FAO papers to psoriasis biology is taken for granted, and that weakens the scientific grounding.

Then, without any real transition, the authors suddenly move into **innate immune cells**—neutrophils, dendritic cells, macrophages. This whole block reads like it was written for a different review and placed here without adapting it to the FAO/PPARγ narrative. There is no real “crosstalk” shown between fatty-acid metabolism and these innate pathways, which is especially disappointing given the paper’s title.

The clinical section suffers the most. Instead of carefully tying therapeutic ideas to the mechanisms described earlier, it introduces topical SCFAs, probiotics, fibers, bacterial extracts, and various interventions with very little explanation of how they relate to the rest of the manuscript. Some of these ideas may have merit, but in the current form they feel disconnected and, at times, speculative.

Taken together, the paper reads like **several separate pieces stitched together**, rather than one coherent review. The strong parts on adaptive immunometabolism don’t blend with the abrupt innate-immune section, and the clinical part arrives “out of the blue” with no supporting framework.

The authors clearly put effort into collecting interesting studies, but the manuscript needs major reconstruction before it can be considered. A clearer title, stronger focus, and a unified narrative would help greatly.

If you want, I can tune this to be **sharper**, **more diplomatic**, or more like a **slightly annoyed senior reviewer**—just tell me the tone.

# **Reviewer Comment (Frontiers-Level, Academic, Human)**

The manuscript entitled **“Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk in Psoriasis”** presents an ambitious topic, and several sections demonstrate careful reading of the immunometabolic literature. However, the manuscript as a whole does not succeed in delivering a coherent or conceptually unified review.

The central difficulty begins with the title. The authors promise an examination of fatty-acid–driven interactions with the *innate* immune system in psoriasis, yet the manuscript devotes most of its space to **adaptive immunology**, particularly the regulation of the Treg/Th17 axis through **PPARγ-dependent fatty acid oxidation (FAO)**. These sections are reasonably detailed and show some rigor, but they are not aligned with the stated focus, and this mismatch sets the reader on the wrong trajectory from the outset.

A second issue concerns the use of the literature. Much of the foundational material derives from **oncology, metabolic disease, or general immunology**, with only a modest fraction of references actually involving psoriasis. This is not inherently problematic, but it *is* problematic that the manuscript does not acknowledge these extrapolations or justify them. As written, it is often unclear which conclusions are psoriasis-based and which are inferred from unrelated systems.

The subsequent shift into **innate immune mechanisms**—neutrophils, dendritic cells, macrophages—arrives abruptly and without conceptual preparation. These sections read as an independent review, not the continuation of a metabolic narrative. No genuine “crosstalk” is demonstrated between FAO/PPARγ biology and innate immune pathways, leaving the reader with the impression that two different manuscripts have been combined without synthesis.

The section on **therapeutic applications** exacerbates this fragmentation. Rather than translating the mechanistic framework into plausible interventions, the authors present a list of topical SCFAs, probiotics, dietary fibers, and related strategies with minimal explanation of how these approaches emerge from the pathways discussed earlier. The clinical content feels disconnected and, at times, speculative.

Taken together, the manuscript suffers from a structural lack of integration: a title that points in one direction, extensive adaptive immunometabolism that points in another, an isolated treatment of innate immunity, and a set of clinical suggestions that do not follow logically from either. Although individual components show effort, the review, in its current state, does not form a coherent whole and would require substantial reorganization and reconceptualization before it could be considered suitable for publication.

---

- a **numbered list of major revision points**,  
- a **formal editor-facing summary**, or  
- a **revised structure for the manuscript** aligned with your reference architecture.

Current abstract overpromises integration and “crosstalk” that the manuscript doesn’t deliver.

You need an abstract that:

- clearly says:  
  - psoriasis → Treg/Th17 axis  
  - PPARγ → FAO → Tregs/Th17 balance  
  - SCFAs → Tregs + innate cells via receptors/HDAC

Write a new abstract that reflects the *actual* narrative, not the original title fantasy.

## 2. Rewrite the Introduction so it doesn’t lie

Your current introduction:

- does a good job on Treg/Th17 + FAO  
- does a bad job connecting PPARγ and SCFAs  
- says nothing honest about the limited psoriasis evidence  
- completely ignores that much of the later material is extrapolated

What a **Frontiers-worthy** introduction should do here:

1. **Define psoriasis**: immunometabolic inflammatory disease.  
2. **Introduce Treg/Th17 axis** *and* IL-23/IL-17 axis briefly.  
3. **Introduce metabolism as a decision-maker** (FAO vs glycolysis, HDAC, etc.).  
4. Bring in **PPARγ + FAO** as one regulatory node.  
5. Bring in **SCFAs** as a second, microbiota-linked node.  
6. State honestly:

   - “Much of the mechanistic evidence derives from non-psoriatic models (cancer, colitis, systemic immunology), and this review will make these extrapolations explicit.”

7. Finish with a **clear review aim**, e.g.:

   > “In this review, we synthesize current evidence on PPARγ-mediated fatty acid oxidation and SCFA-mediated immunomodulation in the context of psoriatic immunity, highlighting both psoriasis-specific data and mechanistic insights extrapolated from other disease models.”

📌 **Action:**  
Your intro shouldn’t pretend this is a clean psoriasis-only story. It should **frame the review as a mechanistic, partly extrapolative synthesis**.

- 3. FAO in Tregs, Th17, and PPARγ’s role

But you must:

- **flag extrapolations** explicitly:  
  “In cancer models…”, “In colitis…”, “While not shown in psoriasis, these mechanisms suggest…”
- **soften claims**:  
  change “PPARγ is the key regulator” → “PPARγ is a *significant* regulator among others (e.g., PPARδ, AMPK).”
- **snip red references**:  
  remove goats, jojoba oil, MAS receptor, etc. They scream “we padded the bibliography.”

### 3.2. Scientific honesty corrections

- Mention **PPARδ** at least briefly as a FAO-regulating PPAR in T-cell metabolism.
- Clarify that **Tregs are not purely FAO** and Th17 are not purely glycolytic; you’re describing dominant tendencies, not binary switches.
- Avoid implying SCFAs act via PPARγ unless you can back it properly (you mostly can’t).

📌 **Action:**

Right now Section 4 = “everything we know about SCFAs thrown into a blender.”

New framing paragraph at the start:

Then:

- 4.1 SCFA transport and receptors (short, mechanistic)  
- 4.2 SCFAs and Treg/Th17 (link to Sections 2–3)  
- 4.3 SCFAs and innate cells (DCs, macrophages, neutrophils)

### 4.2. Integration instead of listing

Don’t present neutrophils, DCs, macrophages as three separate worlds.  
Show the **logic**:

- DCs and macrophages drive IL-23/IL-12 → Th17  
- SCFAs can make DCs more tolerogenic, macrophages more M2  
- That cascades into Treg/Th17 modulation  
- Neutrophils (NETs, IL-17) might be influenced indirectly via cytokine milieu

Now we’re doing *actual crosstalk*, not copy-paste immunology.

📌 **Action:**

### 5.2. Organize by mechanism, not by random intervention

Structure it like:

- 5.1 **PPARγ-targeting interventions**  
  - thiazolidinediones (acknowledge limitations, safety issues, no real psoriasis trials)  
  - conceptual possibility of more selective PPARγ modulators in skin  
- 5.2 **FAO/AMPK modulation**  
  - metformin data in immune-mediated disease (not psoriasis)  
  - hypothetical relevance

  - topical butyrate in IMQ psoriasis (good)

No cheese, no “maybe SCFAs from food are magically healing.”  
No grand claims.

### 5.3. Be explicit about limits

Frontiers reviewers like this sentence type:

> “At present, there is insufficient human evidence to recommend SCFA-based or PPARγ-targeted therapies in psoriasis outside of controlled research settings.”

📌 **Action:**

This section is where you show you *didn’t* just glue two reviews together; you actually thought about their joint implications.

- Re-summarizes the **two main axes**: PPARγ–FAO and SCFAs.

- States the **limits**: non-psoriatic models, preclinical data, need for human studies.

  - metabolomics and lipidomics in psoriasis,

---

## Overall assessment

This review tackles an important and timely topic at the intersection of immunometabolism and psoriasis. The authors have clearly invested substantial effort in gathering mechanistic data on PPARγ-driven fatty acid oxidation (FAO) and short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs), and parts of the manuscript show reasonable scientific rigor. However, in its current form the article suffers from major problems of scope, structure, and internal coherence.

The title, abstract, and introduction promise a review of *“fatty acid–innate immune crosstalk in psoriasis”*, but the core of the manuscript is actually a review of **adaptive immunometabolism** (PPARγ–FAO–Treg/Th17), followed by a largely separate SCFA/immunology mini-review and a loosely connected therapeutic section. There is no true integration of these pieces into a single conceptual narrative, and the psoriasis focus is diluted by extensive reliance on non-psoriatic literature without sufficient acknowledgment.

Given the quality of some of the mechanistic content, I recommend **major revision**, with a strong emphasis on reconceptualization and restructuring.

---

## Major revision requests

### 1. Align the title and scope with the actual content

- The current title (“Fatty Acid–Innate Immune Crosstalk in Psoriasis”) is misleading.  
  Most of the manuscript deals with:
  - PPARγ-mediated FAO in Treg/Th17 balance (adaptive immunity), and  
  - SCFA-mediated modulation of adaptive and innate responses.
- The work **does not primarily focus** on innate immunity, nor does it adequately demonstrate a fatty acid–innate “crosstalk” in a mechanistically integrated way.
- **Request:** Either:
  - (a) change the title to reflect the actual focus (e.g. metabolic regulation of psoriatic immunity via PPARγ-FAO and SCFAs), **or**
  - (b) substantially rework the manuscript to genuinely center on fatty acid–innate immune crosstalk (particularly in keratinocytes, DCs, macrophages, neutrophils), with adaptive content supporting that central theme.

At present, the title sets expectations the manuscript does not fulfill.

---

### 2. Rebuild the narrative structure for coherence

The manuscript currently reads like three partially independent reviews:

1. Sections 2–3: PPARγ–FAO–Treg/Th17 (adaptive immunometabolism)  
2. Section 4: SCFA immunology (adaptive + innate, mostly non-psoriatic)  
3. Section 5: clinical/therapeutic ideas (topical SCFAs, probiotics, fibers, derivatives)

There is no clear conceptual bridge between these parts.

**Requests:**

- Introduce a **unified conceptual framework** early in the Introduction:  
  e.g., “Two major metabolic axes relevant to psoriatic immunity: (1) endogenous FAO/PPARγ and (2) microbial SCFAs via receptors and epigenetic mechanisms.”
- Restructure the middle of the manuscript so it follows a **logical progression**, for example:
  1. PPARγ and FAO in immune cells (with psoriasis relevance)  
  2. FAO and Treg/Th17 balance  
  3. SCFAs: transport, receptors, and epigenetic effects  
  4. SCFAs in Treg/Th17 modulation and innate immunity  
  5. An explicit **integration section**: how PPARγ/FAO and SCFA pathways converge on common immune axes (Treg/Th17, IL-23/IL-17, DC/macrophage polarization).
- Ensure that each section explicitly links back to **psoriasis** and not just to generic immunology.

---

### 3. Clarify and acknowledge the reliance on non-psoriatic and preclinical literature

A large fraction of the cited work comes from:

- oncology (various cancers),  
- colitis and gut immunology,  
- HIV, SLE, and other autoimmune diseases,  
- generic T-cell activation systems,  
- systemic metabolic disease models.

This is scientifically acceptable for mechanistic explanation, but it must be **transparent**.

**Requests:**

- Clearly distinguish which findings:
  - are psoriasis-specific (human tissue, patients, psoriasis models),  
  - are from other diseases or generic immunology,  
  - are purely mechanistic/biochemical.
- Add a dedicated paragraph (perhaps in the Introduction or Discussion) explicitly stating the **limitations of extrapolating** from non-psoriatic models to psoriasis.
- When presenting mechanistic pathways, **label the source context** (e.g., “In colitis models…”, “In tumor-bearing mice…”, “While not evaluated in psoriasis, these data suggest…”).

This will greatly improve scientific honesty and credibility.

---

### 4. Correct oversimplifications and missing mechanistic players

Several mechanistic aspects are oversimplified or incomplete:

- PPARγ is repeatedly presented as *the* key regulator of FAO in T cells. In reality, **PPARδ** and **AMPK** are also central regulators of FAO in T cells and other immune cells.
- The FAO vs glycolysis dichotomy is framed as:
  - FAO = Treg  
  - Glycolysis = Th17  
  This is useful heuristically, but biologically too binary.
- SCFAs are at times implied to act via PPARγ, which is not strongly supported in the psoriatic context and may mislead readers.

**Requests:**

- Introduce **PPARδ** and AMPK more explicitly as part of the FAO regulatory network, even if the main narrative still focuses on PPARγ.
- Add clarifying sentences to indicate that:
  - Treg and Th17 cells display **dominant metabolic preferences**, not exclusive pathways, and metabolic plasticity exists.
- Avoid implying direct SCFA → PPARγ control unless supported by specific data in relevant immune/skin contexts; otherwise keep SCFA effects framed around **GPCRs and HDAC inhibition**.

These adjustments are important for mechanistic accuracy.

---

### 5. Strongly revise the SCFA and innate immunity sections for integration and focus

Section 4 currently reads like a general SCFA immunology review, with fragments on neutrophils, DCs, and macrophages drawn mostly from gut and systemic contexts.

**Requests:**

- Refocus the SCFA sections to:
  - first explain core mechanisms (transport, receptors, HDAC inhibition),  
  - then highlight how these mechanisms **might intersect with psoriatic pathways** (e.g., IL-23/IL-17 axis, Treg/Th17, NETs, DC-derived IL-12/IL-23).
- When discussing innate cells (neutrophils, DCs, macrophages):
  - emphasize psoriasis-relevant aspects (NETs, IL-23 production, M1/M2 balance),  
  - clearly state when evidence is from **non-psoriatic models**, and avoid presenting it as established psoriasis biology.
- Try to **avoid compartmentalising** SCFAs into three unrelated segments (T cells, neutrophils, DCs, macrophages). Instead, emphasize how SCFA effects on innate cells may feed into T-cell differentiation and cytokine milieu in psoriasis.

---

### 6. Rewrite the clinical/therapeutic section to be mechanistically grounded and cautious

The current clinical section introduces topical SCFAs, SCFA derivatives, probiotics, inulin, and even cheese consumption with limited connection to the mechanistic framework and variable relevance to psoriasis.

**Requests:**

- Organize therapeutic content by **mechanism**, not by “interesting examples”:
  - PPARγ-targeted strategies (e.g., thiazolidinediones – with appropriate caveats),  
  - FAO/AMPK modulators (e.g., metformin in other immune diseases),  
  - SCFA-related interventions (topical butyrate, acetate effects, SCFA derivatives),  
  - microbiome/diet-based strategies (inulin, probiotics).
- Explicitly link each proposed intervention to **the pathways discussed earlier** (PPARγ, FAO, SCFA-GPCR, HDAC, Treg/Th17, innate cell polarization).
- Highlight the **very preliminary nature** of most data:
  - mostly murine (e.g., IMQ models)  
  - very few or no robust human trials in psoriasis.
- Avoid over-interpretation of probiotic and dietary data; clearly label them as **experimental or hypothesis-generating**, not established clinical tools.

This section should read as a careful translational perspective, not as a speculative shopping list.

---

### 7. Add an explicit “integration” or “synthesis” section

Given that the manuscript deals with **two distinct metabolic paradigms** (PPARγ–FAO and SCFAs), there should be an explicit attempt to synthesize them.

**Requests:**

- Add a short section (before the Conclusion) that:
  - recaps PPARγ–FAO and SCFA mechanisms,  
  - discusses where they **converge** (Treg support, Th17 suppression, modulation of IL-23/IL-17 axis, innate cell reprogramming),  
  - acknowledges that this integrated model is partly hypothetical but useful for guiding future research.
- This will help demonstrate a genuine attempt to create a **coherent “immunometabolic” perspective** rather than simply juxtaposing two literatures.

---

## Minor comments

- Some sentences are overly strong in causal language (e.g., “X controls Y”, “SCFAs ameliorate psoriasis”). Please moderate to “X regulates Y in [specific model]”, “SCFAs have been shown to reduce psoriasis-like inflammation in murine models”, etc.
- Consider light language editing for consistency (PPARγ vs PPAR-γ; Th17 vs Th17 cells; FAO vs fatty acid oxidation).
- Figures (if included or planned) should ideally depict:
  - PPARγ/FAO axis,  
  - SCFA axis,  
  - points of convergence (Treg/Th17, IL-17, DCs/macrophages).
- Clearly indicate in legends and text whether a given pathway is **directly shown in psoriasis**, extrapolated from other diseases, or purely mechanistic.

---

## Strengths of the review

- The authors clearly understand **immunometabolic principles**, particularly regarding Treg/Th17 biology and FAO.
- Sections describing PPA
